A value of ~75 nM has been taken as the mean Cmax in humans at MRHD.

The effect of semaglutide on the central nervous system was studied in the rat CNS (Irwin) study. In this study no significant gross behavioural or physiological changes were observed, during the 24 h post-dose period in rats receiving subcutaneous treatment with semaglutide. Abnormal gait (walking on toes), passivity, decreased touch response, increased urination, lethargy and piloerection were observed in animals administered 95 μ g/kg semaglutide, which corresponds to ~0.6 fold the maximal plasma (Cmax) exposure at the maximum recommended human dose (MRHD). The observed effects are considered to be pharmacology related and likely due to the activity at GLP-1 receptors in the CNS. The No Observed Adverse Effect Level (NOAEL) was determined to be 22 μ g/kg.

Semaglutide, given subcutaneously at doses up to 84 μ g/kg, had no statistically significant effects on respiratory rate, tidal volume or minute volume up to 24 hours after dosing in male SD rats.

Treatment with semaglutide (&gt;100-fold higher concentration than the mean maximal plasma concentration at the MRHD) produced no inhibition of hERG channel tail current recorded in HEK293 cells stably transfected with hERG cDNA, nor an effect on action potential parameters in isolated female rabbit Purkinje fibres. This indicates that semaglutide has a low potential for QT prolongation.

The acute effect of semaglutide on cardiovascular function was studied in male conscious unrestrained cynomolgus monkeys equipped with telemetry transmitters and dosed subcutaneously with ascending doses of semaglutide. No effects related to semaglutide were observed on arterial blood pressure (systolic, diastolic and mean) or the lead II ECG variables examined (RR, PR, QR, QTcF and QTcQ intervals or QRS duration). In conclusion, it was found that there were no clinically relevant findings in cynomolgus monkeys in single doses up to 470 μ g/kg (about 6-fold above MRHD based on Cmax).

In addition, in the repeat dose toxicology study at week 13, 26 and 52, the cardiac electrophysiology was monitored by ECG in male and female telemetered cynomolgus monkeys (10, 60 and 360 μ g/kg twice-weekly sc). In this 52-week toxicity monkey study, a left-bundle-branch-block was observed in one female animal at high dose of 360 μ g/kg (~10-fold above MRHD). The animal exhibited no clinical signs attributable to the ECG finding and histopathology revealed no correlating changes. Cardiac bundle-branch blocks are an occasional finding in monkeys and humans and are in most cases a consequence of other underlying cardiac diseases. Although histopathology revealed no changes in the heart, the ECG finding was considered adverse. When heart rate was analysed as change from baseline, it was shown that there seems to be a transient increase in heart rate at week 26 which

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

returns to baseline values at week 52 in males but remains elevated at week 52 in high dose females. This finding supports the increase in heart rate seen in patients in the clinical trials.

A renal function study was performed to evaluate the acute effects of semaglutide on the renal system in the rat. Semaglutide caused an acute transient increase in diuresis during the first 8 hours after dosing at the highest doses (23 and 89 μ g/kg) and a decrease in the diuresis parameters thereafter. These observations are well known effects of GLP-1R agonists in the rat. Acute effects on diuresis have also been shown in humans with native GLP-1 but not following chronic administration of GLP-1R agonists. The NOAEL was determined to be 5 μ g/kg.

## 4.3.1.4. Pharmacodynamic drug interactions

Non-clinical pharmacodynamic drug interaction studies have not been conducted with semaglutide, which is agreed upon. GLP-1R agonists have been reported to delay gastric emptying but this was evaluated in clinical trials.

## 4.3.2. Pharmacokinetics

## 4.3.2.1. Absorption

The pharmacokinetics were dose-proportional and there was no gender dependency. The absorption of semaglutide from the subcutaneous injection site was rapid in mouse and rat, but slower in rabbit, monkey and minipig. The time to maximum concentration (Tmax) was 2 to 3 hours in mouse and rat, and about 24 hours in rabbit, monkey and minipig. The bioavailability ranged from 86% (monkey) to 94% (minipig). In human, the bioavailability was equally high (89%), but the absorption was slower (Tmax 60 h).

The mean dose-normalised concentration was similar in monkey and human, while it was lower in mouse, rabbit and rat due to faster clearance. The terminal half-life was estimated to be 8 h in the mouse, 11 h in the rat, 28 h in the rabbit, 51 h in the monkey and 148 h in human.

The distribution volume was low (0.2 L/kg) following i.v. administration in the monkey, which corresponds approximately to the volume of extracellular water and indicates that a high fraction of semaglutide is circulating in plasma and extracellular fluid.

Comparison of single dose pharmacokinetics in monkey after subcutaneous and intravenous dosing indicated that elimination is not limited by the absorption rate from subcutis.

## 4.3.2.2. Distribution

The pharmacokinetics following repeated dosing of subcutaneous semaglutide showed a linear relationship between doses and exposures. No gender differences were noted. The dose normalised exposure was generally lower for mice, rats, rabbits and minipigs compared to monkeys and humans due to faster clearance. To ensure continued exposure, and to mimic the once-weekly exposure profile in humans, once-daily dosing was used in mice and rats, and twice-weekly dosing was used in monkeys. At these dose intervals, there was no apparent (i.e. &lt; 2-fold) systemic accumulation.

No difference in exposure was observed between pregnant and non-pregnant animals following repeated administration of semaglutide to rats, rabbits and monkeys. However, rabbits showed some accumulation in the embryofoetal development study, but the wide range (1.3 up to 13-fold) and the few data do not permit a clear conclusion.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

## Plasma protein binding

In vitro binding studies showed that the plasma protein binding was high, &gt;99%, and that albumin was the primary protein responsible for binding of semaglutide in plasma. The potential binding to other plasma proteins has not been studied. The fraction unbound was somewhat lower in plasma from mouse, rat and rabbit (0.07-0.28%) as compared to plasma from monkey (0.46%) and human (0.36%).

## Distribution to red blood cells

As determined in rats, whole blood concentrations of semaglutide-related material were approximately half of the values in plasma, suggesting no preferential uptake into red cells.

## Tissue distribution

Distribution studies in rats showed the highest presence of semaglutide-related material in blood and in highly perfused tissues.

After subcutaneous administration of [3H]-Oct- or [3H]-Tyr-labelled semaglutide, the tissue-to-blood ratios of semaglutide related material were generally below 1. The highest levels were associated with lung, tooth pulp, kidney (cortex and medulla), bladder, adrenal medulla and uterus. The high levels in the bile ducts, up to and including 3 days after dosing, suggests that biliary secretion may have played an important role in elimination by contributing to faecal excretion. In addition, the moderate levels of radioactivity present in the kidneys and bladder also suggest that urinary elimination occurred. The lowest concentrations were present in the central nervous system (brain and spinal cord) and white fat.

The distribution and concentrations of [3H]-Oct-semaglutide related material in male pigmented rats were similar to that in male albino rats, suggesting that semaglutide related material does not bind to melanin or accumulate in pigmented tissues.

## Placenta transfer

Semaglutide related material passed the placental barrier in rats and rabbits but distributed to foetal tissue at levels lower than in dam plasma (&lt;4%). This suggests limited distribution across placenta. Nevertheless, a single dose of semaglutide to pregnant rats at GD18, led to low, but measurable levels in foetuses at 24h post dose and effects on the foetus were observed.

## Excretion into milk

Semaglutide and metabolites are excreted into rat milk. Mean concentrations were 3-12 times lower than in plasma up to 24 hours after a subcutaneous dose 0.3 mg/kg/day semaglutide. There are no data on the excretion of semaglutide in human milk. A risk to the newborns/infants cannot be excluded. Semaglutide should not be used during breastfeeding.

## 4.3.2.3. Metabolism

The in vitro metabolism of radiolabelled semaglutide was studied in hepatocytes from rats, monkeys and humans. Limited metabolism was observed in all species, and no unique human metabolites were formed. It was shown that semaglutide is metabolised by proteolytic cleavage of the peptide backbone by neutral endopeptidase (neprilysin) and sequential beta-oxidation of the fatty acid side chain.

The in vivo metabolism of semaglutide was investigated by chromatographic metabolite profiling of plasma, urine and faeces from rat, monkey and human following administration of radiolabelled semaglutide. The metabolite profiles from plasma were similar across species. The peptide backbone of

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

semaglutide was metabolised by proteolytic degradation, and the fatty acid moiety was degraded by sequential beta-oxidation.

Semaglutide was the most abundant component in plasma across animal species, accounting for 6993% of the total amount of semaglutide related material and 4 to 12 metabolites which constituted in total only a small part in relation to the amount unchanged semaglutide.

In human plasma, there were 6 metabolites, each contributing 0.4-7.7% to the total amount of semaglutide-related material, whereas the contribution of unchanged semaglutide was 83%. The largest metabolite (P3) contained at least three components (P3A, P3B and P3C). P3C was characterised as a semaglutide isomer. P3B was identified as a peptide metabolite from semaglutide, following proteolytic cleavage and the loss of the first 13 amino acids. Neprilysin could form the metabolite P3B in vitro . No further structural information could be provided P3A and P3C, due to the limited amounts in plasma. All human metabolites are also present in rats, and P3, P5 and P7 are also present in monkeys.

The two primary metabolites in human (U6 and U7) were identified as the free Lys26 amino acid bound to the ADO-linker with butyric (C4) or hexanoic (C6) di-acid side chains attached. These metabolites are products formed from full proteolytic cleavage of the peptide backbone with sequential removal of C2-units by beta-oxidation of the di-fatty acid side chain. The urine metabolite U22 was identified as semaglutide. Only limited amounts of unchanged semaglutide were observed in urine of animals (1%) and humans (3%).

The pharmacological activity of the metabolites has not been evaluated. These metabolites, such as P3B and P3C, may be pharmacologically active since they have structural similarities with semaglutide. The possible contribution of these metabolites to the pharmacological activity of the final product will be minor, because in plasma they are only a small part in relation to the amount of unchanged semaglutide (&lt; 7.7%).

## 4.3.2.4. Excretion

Semaglutide was extensively metabolised prior to elimination. In human, unchanged semaglutide were observed in small amounts in human urine (3.1%) but was not detected in faeces. In rat and monkey, both urine and faeces were equally important as excretion routes of semaglutide and related material. The contribution of urinary excretion was 37% in rats and 30% in monkey, whereas the contribution of faecal excretion was 35% and 21% in these species, respectively. In human, the urinary excretion was the predominant route of excretion (53%), followed by faeces (18.6%).

In bile-cannulated rats, bile was primary route for excretion of semaglutide-related material into faeces (48%), of which approximately 14% was unchanged semaglutide. Other components in bile were metabolites, each accounting for less than 5% of the administered dose.

## 4.3.2.5. Pharmacokinetic drug interactions

The results of the in vitro and in vivo studies on the drug interaction potential of semaglutide have been evaluated in the clinical assessment report.

## 4.3.2.6. Other pharmacokinetic studies

No other pharmacokinetic studies were reported.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

## 4.4. Toxicology

## 4.4.1. Single-dose toxicity

A single dose up to 12mg/kg (mouse) or 7.532 mg/kg (rat) was generally well tolerated. Observed major findings such as reduced body weight and food intake showed quick recovery and can be related to the pharmacological action of semaglutide.

## 4.4.2. Repeat-dose toxicity

Repeated dose studies in mice, rats and cynomolgus monkeys revealed mainly effects related to the pharmacological action of semaglutide. Reduction in food intake and body weight gain were dose limiting, as exceeding the maximum tolerated dose in monkeys led to dehydration consequently followed by euthanisation. However, dose escalation improves tolerability.

Hypertrophy of Brunner's glands of the duodenum was observed in rats after 26 weeks of treatment. This effect is likely due to the high expression of GLP-1R on Brunner's glands. However, there was no progression to hyper- or neoplasia in the rodent carcinogenicity studies, and no similar observations in cynomolgus monkeys dosed for 52 weeks. Therefore, this observation is not considered a safety concern in humans. Thyroid C-cell hyperplasia was only observed in mice at all dose levels. This is an expected result also seen with other GLP-1 agonists and can be considered a class effect.

The 52-week monkey study revealed a chronic left bundle-branch-block in one high dose female. Although the abnormal ECG was confined to a single animal, the observation was considered adverse.

An increase in uterus fluid distension and luminal dilatation is seen in rats after 26 weeks of dosing. These findings are likely due to differences in the stage of the sexual cycle which could be treatment related, and likely secondary to reduction in body weight. Daily subcutaneous administration to Sprague-Dawley rats over a treatment period of 13 weeks with 0.48 mg/kg/day and 0.45 mg/kg/day semaglutide respectively, demonstrated generally similar observations between two formulations based on two different manufacturing processes and although there were a few minor differences, none was considered of any toxicological significance.

## 4.4.3. Genotoxicity

Semaglutide is not genotoxic in vitro or in vivo .

## 4.4.4. Carcinogenicity

In carcinogenicity studies in mice and rats, thyroid C-cell adenomas and carcinomas were observed at all dose levels. This is an expected result also seen with other GLP-1 agonists and can be considered a class effect. No other tumours were found. Other non-neoplastic effects were secondary to the decreased body weight gain related to the pharmacological action of semaglutide. To determine whether the thyroid C-cell tumours are indeed caused by the same mechanism as is responsible for Ccell tumours observed after treatment with GLP-1 agonists, the applicant performed some mechanistic studies. The activation of the GLP-1R was tested in vitro on a thyroid C-cell tumour cell line and compared to GLP-1, exenatide and liraglutide. It was shown that the potency of semaglutide to activate the receptor was similar to liraglutide, and less potent than GLP-1 and exenatide.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

Increased plasma calcitonin concentration is considered a marker for increased activation of GLP-1R on the thyroid C-cells. Upon chronic activation this leads to up-regulation of calcitonin synthesis and further to C-cell proliferation and tumour formation. Therefore, the applicant performed in vivo studies in mice and rats, which show that even after a single 1 mg/kg dose of semaglutide in mice, plasma calcitonin levels were increased 12 and 24 hours after injection. In rats however, an increase calcitonin level was not seen in females, and not very convincingly in males after 6 weeks of treatment. This could be due to the very short half-life of calcitonin in rats of 4 minutes, or a delayed effect which is still not apparent after 6 weeks. Further, an inconsistent effect on calcitonin levels in rats was also seen for liraglutide. Overall, the mechanism of formation of rodent thyroid C-cell tumours is well known and discussed in the public literature. There is no reason to suggest a different mechanism might be responsible for the C-cell tumours observed after treatment with semaglutide, and therefore the thyroid C-cell tumours are likely rodent specific. Since relevance for humans cannot be completely ruled out, thyroid C-cell tumours are listed in the RMP as potential risk.

## 4.4.5. Developmental and reproductive toxicity

Semaglutide caused embryotoxicity in the rat. The observed effects included embryo-foetal mortality, growth retardation, and skeletal and visceral abnormalities. The effects were observed at dose levels of 0.03 mg/kg/day and above, with AUC exposures below the clinical exposure at the MRHD of 2.4 mg/week. The applicant describes a mechanism of action for the embryotoxic effects observed in the rat reproduction study, which involves the presence of GLP-1R on the yolk sac. Semaglutide binds to the receptors on the yolk sac, leading to inhibition of transport of nutrients across the membrane. This mechanism is likely rat specific, since rat embryos are dependent on the yolk sac for their nutrient supply which is e.g. less important in other species including humans and monkeys. Moreover, GLP-1R is not expressed on monkey yolk sacs.

It is agreed that the mechanism demonstrated is specific for rats and could explain the malformations seen in the rat foetuses. Although undoubtedly this mechanism is responsible for most of the malformations observed, it cannot be excluded that other mechanisms that may not be rat specific are also involved. This is because not only more and other malformations are present, but also foetal weight is much further reduced in embryos of dams treated up to GD17 as compared to GD13. This is after the period (GD12) in which embryos are solely dependent on the yolk sac for nutrition but also rely on the developing chorioallantoic placenta. Although the additional skeletal abnormalities that occur between GD13 and GD17 could still be due to the impaired yolk sac, due to presence of the GLP1R on the rat embryo from GD13.5 and presence of low levels of semaglutide in the foetus as measured on GD20, a direct effect of semaglutide on the foetus, of which the clinical relevance is unknown, cannot be excluded. It appears that a potential direct effect of semaglutide is only relevant in the later stages of pregnancy in rats, since the receptor is not present before GD13.5. Timing of receptor expression, if this is relevant for humans at all, is unknown, but a potential risk for humans is mitigated through the labelling in SmPC section 4.6, where it is stated that semaglutide should not be used during pregnancy and women of childbearing potential should use contraception to avoid unplanned pregnancies. Any further risk mitigation measures are not warranted.

A second embryo-foetal toxicity study was performed in rabbits. Once-daily SC administration of semaglutide to pregnant New Zealand White rabbits markedly reduced maternal body weight and food consumption. This coincided with increased post-implantation losses, incomplete ossification of foetal metacarpals/phalanges, and increased incidences of minor skeletal and visceral foetal abnormalities. The increased post-implantation losses and the foetal pathology findings were possibly secondary to the marked maternal effects, but a direct effect of semaglutide could not be excluded. On the other

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

hand, marked maternal toxicity could also mask a direct effect on the embryo or foetus. Although exposure in the high dose group at GD19 was above the human exposure, it was below human exposure at GD6. The Applicant attributes the observations in the rabbit as described above, primarily to the maternal effects on body weight and food consumption. Delayed ossification observed without concomitant decreases in foetal body weight may warrant increased attention (Carney and Kimmel 2007). However, as the mid and high-dose dams showed lower body weight gains on GD 6-19, and higher than control body weight gains on GD 20-29, any decreased foetal body weights in the mid and high dose groups may have been recovered at termination of the study when the foetal examinations were performed.

Cynomolgus monkeys were used as a third species for embryo-toxicity testing of semaglutide, since monkeys do not rely on a yolk sac for nutrition. In all dose groups, the pregnant females had an initial loss of body weight, and a lower body weight gain as compared to control animals. There were 2 cases of abortion in all dose groups as compared to 1 in the control group. The incidence of 2 out of 16 (12.5%) is close to the incidence of pregnancy loss in cynomolgus monkey controls reported in literature of 11.5% up to GD75 ( Jarvis et al, Birth Defects Research (Part B) 89:175-187 (2010) ).

Further, two major malformations were reported in the study. In the mid-dose group, a single foetus had a fused kidney, and in the high dose group there was one foetus with a misshapen brain. These effects have not previously been reported in historical controls from the same testing site. However, a relevance for humans is unlikely due to the lack of a mechanistic relation to semaglutide and lack of similar findings in other studies. Moreover, any potential risk is mitigated through the labelling in SmPC section 4.6.

There was no effect on postnatal development in offspring of cynomolgous monkeys treated with semaglutide until GD140. Initial maternal body weight losses likely led to an increased incidence of early pregnancy loss and reduced foetal weight in the mid and high dose. No other effects were observed.

A juvenile study was performed where rats from the age of 21 days were dosed for 11 weeks. Apart from general signs of toxicity, sexual maturation and fertility were investigated. Sexual maturation was delayed for both sexes, but this did not coincide with effects on fertility or mating performance. No histopathological findings were noted, and therefore it is considered likely that the delay is due to the decreased body weight gain of the treated animals. No new findings were seen in these juvenile animals that were not seen in the adult animals. This study is of limited relevance in the current procedure, as the indication applied for is in adults only.

## 4.4.6. Toxicokinetics and exposure margins

The average plasma exposure for semaglutide at dose of 2.4 mg/week in subjects with non-alcoholic steatohepatitis was of the same magnitude as the exposure in subjects with obesity. Exposure multiples in pivotal toxicity studies were 9 and 10 in repeated dose studies in rat (26 wk) and monkey (52 wk), resp.; 22 in carcinogenicity study in mouse (2 yr) and 2 in rat (2 yr); 0.4, 0.8, 5 in rat, rabbit, monkey EFD studies resp.; 3 in monkey PPND study; and 8 in rat juvenile toxicity study.

## 4.4.7. Local tolerance

In a local toxicity study in pigs using the subcutaneous route of administration only mild effects related to the vehicle or injection procedure were seen. Further, in all pivotal toxicity studies the subcutaneous route of administration was applied, and therefore local toxicity is considered sufficiently investigated and no concerns for human safety were identified.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

In clinical practice it is possible that the product will be administered by intravenous, intra-arterial or intramuscular routes by mistake. Therefore, possible adverse effects were investigated in rabbits using these routes of administration. No adverse effects were seen other than mild effects related to the vehicle or injection procedure.

## 4.4.8. Other toxicity studies

No dedicated studies were performed. Evaluation of potential antigenicity and immunotoxicity were included in the repeated dose toxicity studies.

## 4.4.9. Ecotoxicity/environmental risk assessment

This application concerns a proposed new indication: treatment of non-cirrhotic metabolic dysfunctionassociated steatohepatitis (MASH) in adults with moderate to advanced liver fibrosis (consistent with stages F2 to F3 fibrosis), which may have an impact on the market penetration factor (FPEN) used in the risk assessment. However, the conclusions of the ERA which has been previously assessed for Ozempic and Wegovy are still valid. The active substance is a peptide, the use of which will not alter the concentration or distribution of the substance in the environment; therefore, it is not expected to pose a risk to the environment.

A bioaccumulation potential is not indicated based on the log KOW &lt; 4.5. A definitive PBT/vPvB assessment is not required.

## 4.5. Overall discussion and conclusions on non-clinical aspects

## 4.5.1. Discussion

## Pharmacodynamics

The company has not conducted any new non-clinical safety studies to support the MASH indication for semaglutide. Instead, the justification relies on the previously established non-clinical safety package generated in the context of the type 2 diabetes (T2D) indication, which was subsequently also used to support the weight management indication. These studies have already undergone regulatory assessment by health authorities globally. To address the pharmacological aspects of the MASH indication, two additional animal pharmacology studies were conducted in NASH mouse models: the diet-induced obesity (DIO-NASH) model and the choline-deficient, amino acid-defined, high-fat diet (CDAA-HFD-NASH) model. Both are well-established in preclinical research.

The applicant submitted data from rodent models demonstrating improved metabolic parameters (e.g. insulin sensitivity, glucose tolerance, body weight reduction), which are presumed to lead to secondary hepatic benefits such as reduced steatosis and inflammation. However, no direct receptor occupancy studies were conducted in animals or human tissues, and the GLP-1R engagement was inferred based on pharmacological class effect and systemic readouts.

Plasma insulin is increased in non-fasted DIO-NASH mice and was strongly reduced by semaglutide to a similar degree as chow reversal. In animals on CDAA-HFD diet, fasting insulin was reduced compared to chow fed animals. After semaglutide treatment, insulin concentrations in plasma were around LLOQ and no effects from semaglutide administration could be measured. In previously assessed non-clinical pharmacology studies, semaglutide stimulated plasma insulin secretion, whereas in non-fasted DIONASH mice plasma insulin was increased and subsequently reduced by semaglutide. No effects on

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

fasting insulin were seen in the CDAA HFD model. Upon request, the applicant elaborated why semaglutide stimulated insulin secretion in a pancreas model and in a beta-cell-reduced Göttingen minipig model, whereas it reduced insulin secretion in a DIO-NASH model and was not affected in the CDAA HFD model. In DIO NASH mice insulin secretion was not measured, but the insulin levels were collected as terminal samples and lower in semaglutide treated animals. This model is characterised by insulin resistance and the low insulin levels in terminal samples might reflect improved insulin sensitivity upon treatment with semaglutide. The results do not indicate that semaglutide inhibits insulin secretion per se. CDAA-HFD mice have enhanced insulin sensitivity and semaglutide might be able to further enhance insulin sensitivity as observed in the DIO NASH model, however, insulin levels were decreased to, or below, the lower limit of quantification and no further changes could be detected, resulting in unaltered insulin levels in the CDAA-HFD model.

Both models have inherent limitations. The DIO-NASH model typically results in only mild-tomoderate fibrosis and requires long-term dietary intervention to achieve liver injury, whereas the CDAA-HFD model fails to reproduce the metabolic alterations that are central to human MASH. Both studies were conducted exclusively in mice, without confirmatory data from a second species or a more translationally relevant disease model.

Given the above, the inclusion of at least one additional pre-clinical model would have been scientifically warranted. For instance, the STAM model or a Western diet-based DIAMOND model could have provided complementary evidence, particularly with regards to fibrosis progression or long-term hepatic outcomes such as hepatocellular carcinoma. A third model would have reduced the potential for model-specific bias, strengthened the translational validity of the non-clinical pharmacological findings, and increased confidence in the relevance of GLP-1 receptor agonism for the treatment of MASH. In the absence of such data, the pharmacological evidence was interpreted with appropriate caution.

Ex vivo studies with human tissue (e.g. precision-cut liver slices or primary hepatocyte cultures) could have strengthened the translational evidence base.

Furthermore, variability in PD responses related to sex, age, or metabolic phenotype was not explored, and no subpopulation analyses were performed.

However, a scientifically sound rationale was provided during assessment for the model selection strategy applied in the non-clinical pharmacology programme. The use of rodent NASH models (GAN DIO-NASH and CDAA-HFD) was well justified based on their translational relevance and alignment with the pathophysiological mechanisms of metabolic and lipotoxic injury that underline human MASH. The reference to the international LITMUS consortium ranking of the GAN DIO-NASH model among the most translationally valid supports this approach. The decision not to include non-rodent species is acceptable, given the limited characterisation and pharmacological responsiveness of nonrodent NASH models at the time of study conception. Furthermore, the ethical justification under the principles of the 3Rs is acknowledged. The availability of confirmatory human data demonstrating consistency with the preclinical findings further supports the translational validity of the programme. The decision not to employ toxin-induced fibros is models (e.g., CCl₄, bile -duct ligation) is scientifically reasonable, as these models do not reproduce the lipotoxic and metabolic components that are central to the pathogenesis of MASH. The applicant's explanation that semaglutide's effects are indirect, mediated through metabolic improvement rather than direct hepatic GLP-1 receptor activation, is consistent with the known pharmacodynamic mechanism of GLP-1 receptor agonists.

Semaglutide failed to reduce steatosis despite significant body weight loss and this apparent disconnect could limit translatability to human NAFLD/MASH. Upon request, a scientifically sound explanation was provided that the lack of steatosis reduction in the CDAA-HFD model is modelintrinsic rather than compound-specific. The CDAA-HFD diet's choline deficiency and reduced

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

methionine content impair VLDL export and fatty-acid oxidation, creating a context in which hepatic triglycerides are effectively 'trapped' and resistant to pharmacologically driven lipid mobilisation. Under these conditions, the absence of a steatosis signal is expected and does not contradict pharmacodynamic benefit.

The argument that semaglutide's MASH improvements may arise indirectly (e.g., via weight loss, improved insulin sensitivity and inflammation) rather than through direct hepatocellular GLP-1 receptor activity is consistent with the known mechanism of GLP-1 receptor agonists. Importantly, the applicant indicated that in the GAN DIO-NASH model-considered more permissive for lipid turnoversemaglutide reduces hepatic lipids, aligning with observations in patients. This cross-model pattern adequately reconciles the apparent disconnect and supports translational validity.

In the data on plasma biomarkers of liver injury and inflammation; ALT, AST, TNF alpha, and MCP-1, it is stated that the loss of statistical significance at 12 weeks is attributed to enzyme decline in the vehicle group. Upon request, it was adequately clarified that baseline ALT/AST data were not collected due to the study design, which involved only terminal sampling. While the absence of within-animal longitudinal enzyme data precludes a direct statistical confirmation of the proposed interpretation, the explanation provided is scientifically reasonable and consistent with known temporal enzyme kinetics in the CDAA-HFD model. The assumption that all groups had comparable baseline ALT/AST values prior to dosing is acceptable, given that all animals were on the same diet regimen and randomised before treatment initiation. The reported early decline in ALT/AST after six weeks in semaglutidetreated animals versus the later spontaneous decline in the vehicle group provides a plausible mechanistic explanation for the observed loss of statistical significance at week 12. Furthermore, the reference to external published data ( Matsumoto et al., 2013 ) supports the interpretation that liver enzyme levels naturally peak around six weeks and then decrease thereafter in this dietary model, lending credibility to the applicant's rationale. The observed temporal dynamics are thus consistent with expected model behaviour and do not undermine the interpretation of semaglutide's early pharmacodynamic effect.

In reply to a question raised during previous EMA Scientific Advice, the applicant indicates in the nonclinical overview that the mechanism for the beneficial effects in MASH is not only due to removal of hepatic lipids (direct effect), but also involves several distinct mechanisms resulting in reduction of markers of liver injury (indirect effect) and fibrosis markers.as supported by the non-clinical data in two NASH models in mice. In addition, mechanisms underlying the effect of semaglutide on MASH were investigated by analysis of the liver transcriptome, collagen expression and collagen turnover. The Applicant provided a scientific discussion about how semaglutide directly and indirectly modulates MASH. In MASH, insulin resistance in hepatic and adipose tissues alongside enhanced hepatic de novo lipogenesis increases lipolysis in the adipose tissue, because the inhibitory effect of insulin on fat cells is reduced. The increased lipolysis promotes the accumulation of lipids in the liver and delivers an excessive amount of toxic lipid species which may cause cellular stress and inflammation. Semaglutide treatment improves insulin resistance thereby restoring the metabolic balance in the liver and adipose tissue. Furthermore, though the GLP1 receptor is not expressed in liver, central GLP1R activation by semaglutide decreases circulating levels of TNF α via adrenergic and opioid receptor signalling and attenuates systemic inflammation and contributing to the beneficial effects of semaglutide on liver fibrosis. Given the lack of GLP1 receptor expression in the liver of both humans and mice, the effects on MASH are considered indirect. These indirect effects are likely mediated both by the weight-loss per se and by weight-loss-independent mechanisms.

The current non-clinical data does not discern weight-loss-dependent from weight-loss-independent effects as weight-matched control groups, i.e. a group in which food intake was reduced to induce a weight loss identical to semaglutide, were not included. Thus, a direct effect of semaglutide has not been demonstrated in NASH/MASH models.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

## 4.5.2. Conclusions

In conclusion, the non-clinical pharmacodynamic tests support for the MASH indication of semaglutide is primarily based on legacy T2D and obesity data. Two murine models (GAN DIO-NASH and CDAAHFD) were used to explore semaglutide's activity in MASH. The model selection and supporting justification substantiate the metabolic and antifibrotic findings within the intended translational context. Other models including non-rodent species do not reproduce the lipotoxic and metabolic components that are central to the pathogenesis of MASH. Additional receptor occupancy studies would have strengthened confidence in GLP-1 receptor agonism as a treatment strategy for MASH.

Semaglutide treatment improved insulin resistance thereby restoring the metabolic balance in the liver and adipose tissue and has attenuated systemic inflammation by decreasing circulating levels of TNF α, thereby contributing to the beneficial effects of semaglutide on liver fibrosis. Hence, semaglutide's effects in MASH are indirect, mediated through metabolic improvement rather than direct hepatic GLP-1 receptor activation, what is consistent with the known pharmacodynamic mechanism of GLP-1 receptor agonists. A direct effect of semaglutide has not been demonstrated in NASH/MASH models.

The pharmacokinetic program including the toxicokinetic analysis are considered sufficient. No major objection or any other concerns were raised.

Considering that semaglutide is an approved product, it was agreed that no further non-clinical toxicology studies were needed.

Overall, there are no objections from a non-clinical point of view to the marketing authorisation of Kayshild.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

## 5. Clinical aspects

## 5.1. Introduction

## 5.1.1. Good Clinical Practice (GCP) aspects

The clinical trials were performed in accordance with GCP as claimed by the applicant

The applicant has provided a statement to the effect that clinical trials conducted outside the community were carried out in accordance with the ethical standards of Directive 2001/20/EC.

Based on the review of clinical data, CHMP did not identify the need for a GCP inspection of the clinical trials included in this dossier ( see section 2.4.3 ).

## 5.1.2. Tabular overview of clinical trials

The present application is based on a clinical pharmacology study 4381, a dose-finding phase 2 trial 4296, an interim evaluation of the pivotal phase 3 trial 4553 at week 72, and a supportive trial 4492 in F4b patients.

Non-clinical and clinical information from previous clinical development programmes for semaglutide for the treatment of T2D (Ozempic) and for weight management (Wegovy) also support the present application.

Data from the cardiovascular outcomes trial (CVOT) SELECT (trial ID EX9536-4388) have also been included.

Population pharmacokinetic (PK) analysis was made based on phase 2 and phase 3 data in patients with MASH and F1 to F4 with regards to age, gender, race, and ethnicity.

There are limited data from the use of semaglutide for MASH in pregnant women.

## 5.2. Clinical pharmacology

## 5.2.1. Methods

Semaglutide was analysed in plasma using plasma protein precipitation followed by LC-MS/MS. The LC-MS/MS bioanalytical assay for semaglutide was the same as used in previous semaglutide applications. Performance of the assay in the PK analysis for the new clinical studies was satisfactory, with ISR demonstrating robustness of the assay.

Analysis of anti-semaglutide antibodies was performed using a tiered approach with screening analysis in tier 1, confirmation analysis in tier 2, and cross-reactivity to endogenous GLP-1 in tier 3. In study 4553 tier 3 also included titre determination of the confirmed antibody positive samples. The assays were validated according to current EMA guidelines. The neutralising anti-semaglutide antibody as well as the neutralising anti-GLP-1 antibody analysis were the same as used previously in the Wegovy program.

## 5.2.2. Pharmacokinetics

## 5.2.2.1. Introduction

Descriptive PK data of semaglutide in subjects with metabolic dysfunction-associated steatohepatitis

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

(MASH) were provided, to support the use of semaglutide subcutaneous (SC) 2.4 mg once weekly (OW) as a treatment option in this population. The clinical pharmacology programme for semaglutide 2.4 mg as a treatment option for MASH builds also upon the clinical development programmes for:

- type 2 diabetes (T2D) (NN9535) with semaglutide SC 0.5, 1.0 mg (Ozempic),
- weight management (NN9536) with semaglutide 2.4 mg (Wegovy).

## 5.2.2.2. Evaluation and qualification of models

The popPK analysis in the MASH population was based on phase 2 studies (4296, 4492) and phase 3 study (4553) including subjects with biopsy-defined MASH and fibrosis stage (F1-F4) characterisation.

A one-compartment structural model with first-order absorption and first-order elimination was used to describe the pharmacokinetics of semaglutide. Between-subject variability was included for CL/V and V/F with a full covariance matrix. Residual variability was described by a proportional error model.

Bodyweight was included on CL/F and V/F based on previous results in clinical pharmacology studies. Other covariates (i.e. sex, age, race, ethnicity, body weight, MASH fibrosis stage, dosing device, renal function, injection site and glycaemic status) were predefined to be analysed using a 'full covariate model' and assumed a power model structure. In addition to the effects of covariates investigated in the popPK model, the effects of anti-semaglutide antibody status (presence or absence of semaglutide antibodies) on the exposure were evaluated.

A reduced population pharmacokinetic model was used for simulations by omitting non-significant covariates based on backwards elimination (p&lt;0.001).

The final reduced popPK model provided a good fit of the semaglutide exposure data in the 2 MASH patient phase 2 clinical Studies 4296, 4492 and phase 3 Study 4553. The model was validated by GOF and VPC plots.

The popPK parameters of the reduced popPK model are given in Table 6.

## 5.2.2.2.1. Population pharmacokinetics

Effects of prespecified covariates on semaglutide exposure were evaluated in the popPK analysis on Studies 4553, 4492 and 4296. For results on covariates see section 5.2.2.10 Special populations .

## 5.2.2.3. Absorption

The maximum concentration was reached 1 to 3 days post dose. Steady state exposure was achieved following 4-5 weeks of once weekly administration. The steady state exposure of semaglutide increased proportionally with doses from 0.25 mg up to 2.4 mg once weekly. Steady state exposure was stable with time as assessed up to week 68. Similar exposure was achieved with SC administration of semaglutide in the abdomen, thigh, or upper arm. The absolute bioavailability of semaglutide was 89%. No influence of food is expected since this concerns a SC administration.

## 5.2.2.4. Bioequivalence

Based on findings from the Wegovy MAA, bioequivalence between the two semaglutide drug products (semaglutide dosed in the DV3396 single-dose pen-injector and a PDS290 pen-injector) was demonstrated for AUC0 168h and Cmax at steady state, both at the 2.4 mg and 1.0 mg dose level.

## 5.2.2.5. Distribution

The mean volume of distribution of semaglutide following subcutaneous administration in patients with MASH was approximately 13.7 L. Semaglutide was extensively bound to plasma albumin (&gt;99%).

## 5.2.2.6. Metabolism

It is known that prior to excretion, semaglutide is extensively metabolised through proteolytic cleavage of the peptide backbone and sequential beta-oxidation of the fatty acid side chain. The enzyme neutral endopeptidase (NEP) was identified as one of the active metabolic enzymes.

The primary excretion routes of semaglutide-related material are via the urine and faeces. ## 5.2.2.8. Dose proportionality and time dependency

Semaglutide dose-proportionality was demonstrated up to 2.4 mg upon once weekly SC administration. Further, the PK of semaglutide is not time-dependent. Steady state is reached after approximately 25 weeks, following the proposed 16-week titration schedule.

## 5.2.2.9. Pharmacokinetics in the target population

Based on popPK simulations, semaglutide exposure in MASH patients at a 2.4 mg dose is comparable with exposure obtained in obese subjects, either with or without T2D. Of note, a large proportion of MASH patients is obese and has T2D, and in that respect the MASH population and the obese and T2D populations for the Ozempic and Wegovy indication overlap significantly.

Effects of prespecified covariates on semaglutide exposure were evaluated in the popPK analysis on Studies 4553, 4492 and 4296 (Figure 2).

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

The covariate analysis demonstrated that body weight affected semaglutide exposure; a 26% lower exposure was predicted in subjects with a high body weight (140 kg, the 95% percentile of the data set) and a 53% higher exposure was predicted in subjects with a low body weight (62 kg, the 5% percentile of the data set), relative to a subject with a prespecified reference body weight (100 kg).

Other covariates (gender, age, race, ethnicity, injection site, MASH fibrosis stage, diabetes status, device, renal and hepatic function, anti-semaglutide antibodies) had minor or no influence on the exposure of semaglutide. This is in line with conclusion in the Ozempic and Wegovy MAA patient populations. The current clinical development programme in MASH did not include any paediatric patients. This is in line with the agreed PIP.

The results of the popPK analysis on Studies 4553, 4492 and 4296 in the population of subjects with MASH are comparable with the results of the corresponding analyses performed for semaglutide 2.4 mg in subjects with overweight or obesity (see Wegovy   MAA).

## 5.2.2.11. PK drug-drug interactions

Semaglutide is not a substrate for CYP enzymes, therefore no interactions affecting semaglutide are expected by that route.

In addition, semaglutide does not have any effect on the activity of CYP enzymes and transporters in vitro at clinically relevant concentrations and is therefore not expected to cause any clinically relevant drug-drug-interactions by that means. Semaglutide delays gastric emptying and could potentially influence the absorption of concomitantly administered oral medicinal products. However, no clinically relevant effect on the rate of gastric emptying was observed with semaglutide 2.4 mg. The proposed text for section 4.5 is identical to that for Wegovy, which was acceptable.

## 5.2.2.12. Interconversion and PK of metabolites

Interconversion . One semaglutide isomer P3C has been identified. The isomer is present in low concentrations (&lt;7.7%). As P3C is a semaglutide isomer, it is considered likely that it has some activity. However, as its concentration is low (&lt;7.7%), the contribution to the total activity is expected to be low.

PK of metabolites . All metabolites accounted for less than 10% of the total amount of semaglutide related material and are not expected to have any activity.

Consequences of genetic polymorphism . The pharmacokinetics data do not indicate any influence of polymorphisms of NEP on the pharmacokinetics of semaglutide.

Intra- and inter-individual variability . The within- and between subject of 17% and 22%, respectively, obtained in the MASH patient popPK model was comparable to that estimated in the Wegovy MAA in obese patients with or without T2D.

## 5.2.3. Pharmacodynamics

## 5.2.3.1. Mechanism of action

Semaglutide is a glucagon-like peptide-1 (GLP-1) analogue with 94% homology to human GLP-1. It is classified as a GLP-1 receptor agonist (RA).

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

The PD properties of semaglutide are well-characterised based on findings from the Ozempic and Wegovy clinical development programmes.

The effects of semaglutide in the body are mediated via specific activation of the GLP-1 receptor and are generally well characterised. Both native GLP-1 and GLP-1 RAs reduce body weight by lowering energy intake via inducing feelings of satiety and fullness and lowering feelings of hunger. The mechanism of decreased energy intake involves GLP-1 receptors expressed in the pancreas and the brain in regions that regulates satiety and appetite. Semaglutide lowers blood glucose by a mechanism that increases insulin secretion and reduces glucagon secretion, both depending on the level of glucose in blood. Semaglutide lowered fasting triglyceride and very-low-density lipoprotein (VLDL) cholesterol blood levels. In addition, semaglutide reduced the postprandial triglyceride and VLDL cholesterol response to a high fat meal.

GLP-1 receptors are also expressed in the heart, blood vessels, as well as on kidney cells and cells of the immune system.

The GLP-1 receptor has not been detected on liver cells and the effects of semaglutide on the liver are thought to be indirect and largely mediated by improvement in metabolic factors, including body weight, blood pressure and inflammation, as well as glucose and lipid metabolism.

Any further detailed underlying mechanism for the improvement in MASH has not been described.

## 5.2.3.2. Primary and secondary pharmacology

The pharmacodynamics were evaluated in the phase 2 study 4381, the dose finding study 4296, and the pivotal phase 3 study 4553. The results of study 4381 are discussed below, while the results of the dose finding and pivotal study are discussed in their respective sections.

Study 4381 primary pharmacology evaluation was based on pharmacodynamic imaging (liver stiffness, liver steatosis, liver fat volume, visceral adipose tissue) and pharmacodynamic laboratory assessment (ALT, AST, GGT, several exploratory markers). Secondary pharmacology was based on pharmacodynamic laboratory assessment (hsCRP, glucose related evaluation (e.g. HbA1c), lipid profile e.g. LDL-C) and body measurements (height, body weight, BMI, and waist circumference).

## Primary pharmacology

- Pharmacodynamic liver imaging

At week 24, 48 and 72, no statistically significant changes from baseline in liver stiffness between semaglutide 0.4 mg and placebo were found. The estimated odds of achieving an at least 15% reduction in liver stiffness did not differ statistically significantly between the semaglutide 0.4 mg and the placebo at weeks 24, 48, or 72.  However, the estimated odds of achieving an at least 30% reduction in relative liver fat content were statistically significantly greater with semaglutide 0.4 mg than with placebo at weeks 24, 48, and 72 (73.5% vs 33.3% at week 72, p=0.0006).

At week 72, the reduction in liver fat content was statistically significantly greater with semaglutide 0.4 mg (- 58%; ratio to baseline of 0.42) than with placebo (-17%; ratio to baseline of 0.83) with an ETR of 0.5 [0.39; 0.66] 95%CI.

At week 72, the reduction in liver fat volume was statistically significantly greater with semaglutide 0.4 mg (-67%; ratio to baseline of 0.33) than with placebo (-20%; ratio to baseline of 0.80) with an ETR of 0.41 [0.30; 0.56] 95%CI.

BMI, body weight, and waist circumference decreased statistically significantly from baseline to week 48 and remained stable to week 72 with semaglutide 0.4 mg, while with the placebo all parameters remained near baseline values (Figure 4).

- HbA1c, FPG, Fasting glucagon and HOMA-IR in subjects with T2D

At week 72, the decrease from baseline in HbA1c was statistically significantly greater with semaglutide 0.4 mg (1.03%) than with placebo (0.03%).

At week 72, the decrease from baseline in FPG observed with semaglutide 0.4 mg (1.74 nmol/L) was statistically significantly greater than with placebo (0.28 nmol/L). At week 72, the reduction in fasting glucagon was statistically significantly greater with semaglutide 0.4 mg (- 37%; ratio to baseline of 0.63) than with placebo (-9%; ratio to baseline of 0.91). No statistically significant changes to baseline in insulin resistance (measured using the HOMA-IR index) were found.

- HbA1c, FPG, Fasting glucagon and HOMA-IR in subjects without T2D

Reduction from baseline in HbA1c was statistically significantly different between semaglutide 0.4 mg and the placebo at week 24, but not at weeks 48 and 72. At week 24, the decrease from baseline in HbA1c observed with semaglutide 0.4 mg (0.49%) was statistically significantly greater than with placebo (0.03%).

At week 74, the decrease from baseline in FPG observed with semaglutide 0.4 mg (0.69 nmol/L) was statistically significantly different from the increase observed with placebo (0.56 nmol/L). No statistically significant changes to baseline in fasting glucagon were found.

Change from baseline in insulin resistance (measured using the HOMA-IR index) was statistically significantly different at week 48, but not at weeks 24 and 72. At week 48, the decrease in insulin resistance observed with semaglutide 0.4 mg (-50%; ratio to baseline of 0.50), was statistically significantly different from the increase observed with placebo (+10%; ratio to baseline of 1.10).

- QT prolongation

As part of the Ozempic ®  clinical development programme, trial NN9535-3652 with semaglutide s.c. 1.5 mg once weekly demonstrated that semaglutide s.c. does not prolong the QTc interval. This conclusion was considered applicable for semaglutide 2.4 mg for weight management (Wegovy) and is also considered applicable for semaglutide 2.4 mg for MASH.
