## 5.2.4. Pharmacokinetics/pharmacodynamics (PK/PD)

The effect of several covariates on the effectivity and safety parameters

- resolution of steatohepatitis and no worsening of liver fibrosis,
- improvement in liver fibrosis and no worsening of steatohepatitis,
- improvement in liver fibrosis,
- progression of liver fibrosis,
- body weight
- gastro-intestinal adverse event,
- vomiting events, and
- nausea events

was investigated. Overall, intrinsic factors like e.g., gender, baseline body weight, diabetes status and fibrosis stage did not appear to have a relevant impact on the two main efficacy or safety parameters.

Data from phase 2 Study 4296 and phase 3 Study 4553 (interim evaluation) showed an exposureresponse relationship for the evaluated primary efficacy outcomes Resolution of steatohepatitis and no worsening of liver fibrosis and Improvement in liver fibrosis and no worsening of steatohepatitis , and secondary confirmatory endpoints (week 72) Resolution of steatohepatitis and improvement in liver fibrosis and Change in body weight . For all these efficacy parameters, increasing exposure was associated with increased efficacy for endpoints related to improved liver histology and body weight loss.

Further, an exposure-dependent increase in the incidence of gastrointestinal AEs, nausea and vomiting was found with increasing exposure. Results are in line with results obtained in the Wegovy MAA.

The exposure-response analyses are considered supportive for the recommended dose of semaglutide for MASH is 2.4 mg.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

## 5.2.5. Dose selection and therapeutic window

The dosing regimen was selected based on an integrated evaluation of efficacy, safety, and exposure considerations from the phase 2 dose-finding Study 4296. The dose was also selected based upon the data from the Ozempic T2D MAA investigating semaglutide SC at doses up to 2.0 mg as well as the Wegovy weight management MAA conducted for semaglutide SC 2.4 mg.

Initially, a once daily dose of 0.1 mg, 0.2 mg, and 0.4 mg was investigated in support of the MASH indication. This once daily administration in trial 4296 was chosen based on the initial data from the Ozempic semaglutide SC T2D development programme suggesting that fluctuations in semaglutide plasma concentrations increased gastrointestinal side effects. Later data from the semaglutide SC Ozempic T2D development programme did not support the hypothesis that once daily dosing provides better gastrointestinal tolerability as compared to once weekly dosing. The proportion of participants with gastrointestinal AEs and the rates of such events were generally higher in Ozempic Study NN9535-4191 where semaglutide SC was dosed once daily compared to the phase 3a studies where semaglutide SC was dosed once weekly. Therefore, based on this sequence of information, the choice for once weekly dosing is supported (and in line with that accepted during the Ozempic and Wegovy MAA).

Dose-response evaluation based on the phase 2 Study 4296 showed a positive relationship between the dose of semaglutide and its effect on resolution of steatohepatitis and no worsening in liver fibrosis at week 72. Semaglutide 0.4 mg once daily led to the highest proportion of subjects achieving resolution of steatohepatitis and no worsening in liver fibrosis. Modelling and Simulation data further suggested levelling off the increase in gastro-intestinal effects at higher doses. The subsequent aim to target this 0.4 mg OD caused exposure with a once weekly dosing is therefore understandable. Based on popPK simulations, the Cmax obtained by 0.4 mg once daily dosing was not passed by a once weekly dosing of 2.4 mg.

Finally, to mitigate gastro-intestinal adverse events, the dose is increased every 4 weeks. The escalation steps applied in the phase 3a Study 4553 (fixed-dose escalation regimen (0.25, 0.5, 1.0, 1.7 mg once weekly)) were identical to the escalation steps for the approved treatment of weight management (Wegovy).

It is considered that the Applicant has provided a reasonable justification for the proposed maintenance dose of 2.4 mg once weekly, to be reached by starting with a dose of 0.25 mg and escalating over a 16-week period to a maintenance dose of 2.4 mg once weekly.

## 5.2.6. Overall discussion and conclusions on clinical pharmacology

## 5.2.6.1. Discussion

## Pharmacokinetics

Descriptive PK data of semaglutide in subjects with metabolic dysfunction-associated steatohepatitis (MASH) (NN9931) were provided, to support the use of semaglutide subcutaneous (SC) 2.4 mg once weekly as a treatment option in this population. The clinical pharmacology programme for semaglutide 2.4 mg as a treatment option for MASH builds also upon the clinical development programmes for:

- type 2 diabetes (T2D) (NN9535) with semaglutide SC 0.5, 1.0 mg (Ozempic),
- weight management (NN9536) with semaglutide 2.4 mg (Wegovy).

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

Most PK data for semaglutide have already been established based on data from the Ozempic and Wegovy MAA. The PK in MASH patients is not different from the PK observed in healthy subjects, as well as subjects with diabetes or in obese patients.

No difference was found between AUC and Cmax following single subcutaneous dose of 0.5 mg semaglutide between individuals with normal hepatic function and those with mild, moderate, or severe hepatic impairment (classified by Child-Pugh criteria). The fraction unbound of semaglutide was less than 0.5% for all subjects but appeared to increase with increasing degree of hepatic impairment. Since the Child-Pugh classification is primarily used to assess the severity of cirrhosis (F4 fibrosis) and is not typically applied to earlier stages of fibrosis (F0, F1, F2, F3), SmPC section 4.2 distinguishes patients with preserved hepatic function (as studied in trial 4553) and those with moderate or severe hepatic impairment, for whom cautionary statements are needed.

## Bioanalysis

The Applicant employed validated LC-MS/MS methods for semaglutide quantification in plasma, consistent with those used in previous submissions such as for Wegovy. These methods demonstrated acceptable performance across multiple clinical trials, with high assay acceptance rates, consistent precision and accuracy, confirmed reproducibility, and no matrix interference, including in lipemic samples.

Immunogenicity was assessed using a multi-tiered approach (screening, confirmatory, cross-reactivity, and neutralisation), also consistent with prior submissions. While the ADA and binding assays were appropriately validated, the neutralizing antibody (NAb) assay showed limited drug tolerance, detecting only high ADA levels in the presence of semaglutide concentrations well below steady-state levels (50-100 nM). This limitation was already noted in earlier regulatory assessments and has not been resolved, but clinical data suggest that detected ADAs were transient and had no apparent impact on pharmacokinetics, efficacy, or safety. Therefore, despite the NAb assay's inability to detect low-titre antibodies during treatment, the overall assay strategy was considered acceptable.

## Pharmacodynamic markers

The MASH (NN9931) program incorporated a broad panel of pharmacodynamic (PD) markers across trials 4381, 4296, and 4553 to non-invasively assess hepatic steatosis, fibrosis, and features relevant to MASH, with varying sensitivity, specificity, and clinical relevance. Among imaging markers, MRIPDFF is the most sensitive and specific for quantifying liver fat content (steatosis), with a strong correlation to histologic steatosis and established use as a surrogate endpoint; however, it does not capture inflammation or hepatocellular ballooning, limiting its ability to define steatohepatitis. Magnetic Resonance Elastography (MRE) provides a highly accurate measure of liver stiffness and is superior to transient elastography for assessing fibrosis, though stiffness may also reflect inflammation. FibroScan, using transient elastography and CAP, is less accurate than MRI-based methods, particularly in obese subjects, but remains a widely accepted tool with utility in evaluating liver stiffness and fat content. The FAST score, which combines FibroScan stiffness, CAP, and AST, provides a validated composite marker for identifying at-risk MASH and monitoring treatment response. Among blood-based markers, ALT, AST, GGT, and hsCRP provide general indicators of liver injury and inflammation but lack disease specificity. Composite indices like FIB-4 and NAFLD fibrosis score (NFS) offer non-invasive estimations of fibrosis based on clinical and biochemical data, but with moderate diagnostic accuracy. The ELF test, which directly measures fibrosis-related proteins (HA, PIIINP, TIMP-1), shows good specificity for advanced fibrosis and is associated with progression to cirrhosis; a decrease of ≥0.5 is considered clinically meaningful. Pro-C3, a marker of collagen III formation, is closely associated with active fibrogenesis in MASH and may reflect treatment impact on fibrotic activity. Exploratory biomarkers such as miR-122, FGF-21, MCP-1, and TNF-alpha may provide mechanistic insights but lack sufficient validation for use as endpoints. Overall, MRI-PDFF and MRE remain the most robust PD tools for

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

assessing steatosis and fibrosis respectively, while blood-based fibrosis markers (e.g. ELF, Pro-C3) and composite scores (e.g. FAST) offer supportive, clinically relevant information. However, none of these markers alone can definitively diagnose or stage MASH which still requires histologic assessment; thus, these PD tools are best interpreted as complementary measures of disease activity and treatment response in MASH and fibrosis.

## popPK/PD

Efficacy evaluation is based on two pivotal studies: one phase 3a trial (study 4553) in patients with MASH F2-F3, and one phase 2 dose-finding trial (study 4296) in patients with MASH F1-F3. These are discussed in detail in the clinical efficacy section below and served as the source data for the main POP-PK/PD report. Study 4492 was excluded from the POP-PK/PD analysis due to differences in population inclusion criteria (e.g. MASH fibrosis stage). Study 4381 was excluded due to a lack of biopsy-defined MASH fibrosis stage characterisation. Nevertheless, both non-pivotal phase 2 studies (4381 and 4492) indicate a significant metabolic benefit with semaglutide but limited direct antifibrotic effect within the study duration.

Two PopPK/PD reports were submitted. They are referred to as the first (or earlier) and the second (interim) report, as they lack report numbers. The earlier report (2020) is based on phase 2 Studies 4296 and 4381. The first report included 2,266 observations from 272 subjects. The PK data set of the second interim report is based on Studies 4553, 4492, and 4296, and includes 811 subjects and 5450 PK samples while the PK/PD data set includes only data from Studies 4553, and 4296 or only 4553.

Both reports used the same computational approach and produced largely overlapping results. A onecompartment model with first-order absorption and elimination was used to describe semaglutide PK. Given the limitation of sparse sampling, this was a reasonable choice. Body weight was the most important covariate for semaglutide exposure, with higher weight resulting in lower exposure. Other covariates-sex, age, race, ethnicity, MASH fibrosis stage, dosing device, renal function, diabetes status, and injection site-were of minimal or no importance. Weight-tiered dosing is not advised, as no clear concentration-effect relationship has been established for efficacy or safety. Instead of observed Ctrough values, model-predicted average concentrations (Cave) were used to explore concentration-effect relationships. Fitted and observed Ctrough values showed strong agreement, justifying the use of model-predicted Cave.

With respect to the popPK model, a number of insignificant covariates (i.e. with 1.0 included in the 95% CI) appear still included in the reduced popPK model. The IIV and shrinkage in the full covariate model and the reduced model were very much comparable. Moreover, the point estimates of the parameter estimates were identical in the full and the reduced models. The popPK model is comparable to that developed in the Ozempic MAA. Further optimisation of the model was not requested.

With respect to the therapeutic window, the applicant argued that efficacy was demonstrated irrespective of body weight, without worsening of liver fibrosis or steatohepatitis. The applicant proposed not to define cut-off exposures leading to significantly reduced efficacy or increased safety issues for semaglutide. In that sense, the exposure-efficacy relation provided for steatohepatitis and no worsening of liver fibrosis is indicative for noticeable improvement of steatohepatitis also following lower doses with corresponding lower semaglutide exposures. It is agreed that from these data it is not feasible to define a clear exposure cut-off leading to unacceptable low efficacy. In relation to safety, it is agreed that the exposure-safety relation is relatively flat, so no marked increase in safety issues is generally noted upon increases exposure beyond a certain level, obtained with the final 2.4 mg dose. It is acknowledged that this also hinders defining a cut-off exposure which leads to unacceptable toxicity. Generally, the reason for asking a therapeutic margin to be established is that this can be used to evaluate e.g. DDI's or organ function impairment potentially affecting semaglutide. In this case, it is reassuring that no DDIs have been reported leading to markedly different semaglutide exposures.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

Likewise, renal or hepatic impairment only have a minor effect on semaglutide exposures. It was agreed that there was no need for defining the therapeutic width for semaglutide.

The covariate analysis demonstrated that body weight affected semaglutide exposure; a 26% lower exposure was predicted in subjects with a high body weight (140 kg, the 95% percentile of the data set) and a 53% higher exposure was predicted in subjects with a low body weight (62 kg, the 5% percentile of the data set), relative to a subject with a prespecified reference body weight (100 kg). Also because an E-R was noted on the MASH efficacy endpoints, the applicant was requested to show that also for the MASH indication the fixed 2.4 mg weekly dose of semaglutide provides an adequate systemic exposure over the body weight range of 42.7-206.0 kg. The applicant provided an evaluation of the weight-response analysis for the primary endpoints steatohepatitis and no worsening of liver fibrosis and liver fibrosis and no worsening of steatohepatitis across subpopulations stratified by baseline body weight (&lt;8 0 kg; ≥80 kg to &lt;100kg; ≥100 kg). In this analysis, effica cy in the highest weight category, with expectedly the lowest exposure, was not significantly different from efficacy reached in lower weight categories with expected higher exposure. The provided information is considered sufficient to conclude that exposure in all weight categories is adequate to obtain efficacy.

## PK/PD results (Study 4296 and 4553)

The two primary categorical composite endpoints-(1) resolution of steatohepatitis with no worsening of fibrosis, and (2) improvement in fibrosis with no worsening of steatohepatitis-were modelled using logistic Emax and linear regression models, respectively, corresponding to the primary clinical endpoints.

There were noticeable differences between the concentration-response curves from Studies 4296 and 4553. These differences theoretically suggest potential variations in efficacy based on dosing frequency-daily dosing in Study 4296 versus weekly dosing in Study 4553. Specifically, semaglutide demonstrated greater effectiveness in resolving steatohepatitis in Study 4553, whereas it was more effective for fibrosis improvement in Study 4296. However, the difference was statistically significant only for fibrosis improvement and was primarily attributed to varying placebo responses across the two studies.

Notably, no clear concentration-dependent effect was observed when assessing fibrosis progression independently. This discrepancy highlights the inherent limitations of composite endpoints, as statistical significance may arise even if improvement occurs predominantly in only one component (e.g., steatohepatitis resolution). Given the relatively short duration of these studies, definitive conclusions regarding semaglutides impact on fibrosis progression are premature. The CHMP Reflection Paper (EMA/CHMP/111529/2024) recommends a study duration of two years prior to interim analysis but provides flexibility dependent on phase 2 data, study size, patient characteristics, and statistical considerations. Further analysis of the exposure-response relationship for liver stiffness (measured by FibroScan) may offer valuable additional insights. Unlike histological evaluations, liver stiffness was assessed at multiple time points (baseline, weeks 24 [Study 4296 only], 48, and 72), which could enhance understanding despite technical limitations. Developing a robust PK/PD model that integrates both concentration and temporal factors could facilitate better prediction of clinical outcomes for Study 4553. The results so far suggest that liver stiffness does not evolve fully in parallel with fibrosis but may serve as a controlling or intermediate factor in disease progression. Under a discrete model framework, liver stiffness could represent a transitional state influencing the probability of progression to the next fibrosis stage. These assumptions are reasonable, but at present no firm conclusions can be drawn regarding the clinical relevance of these markers until on-going trials are completed. Nevertheless, if liver stiffness proves to be a valid biomarker for fibrosis, then the applicant has demonstrated a clear, dose-dependent, and statistically significant effect of semaglutide.

The concentration dependence of adverse effects such as vomiting was modelled using logistic regression without incorporating time.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

## Pharmacodynamics

The applicant has briefly discussed the pharmacodynamic effects of semaglutide as previous characterised from the Ozempic and Wegovy dossiers.

In study 4381, treatment related effects could be observed on relative percentage liver fat content (and subject with a minimum of 30% reduction in liver fat content), liver fat volume, and total liver volume, as measured by MRI. However, no effect could be observed on liver stiffness by MRE (or subjects achieving a minimum of 15% reduction in liver stiffness), liver stiffness by Fibroscan, and on liver synthesis function (albumin, INR). While a treatment effect on the exploratory marker of liver steatosis by Fibroscan could be observed. All these markers examine the effect on associated clinical signs of MASH and on some clinical liver impairment markers. None examine the underlying mechanism of semaglutide.

Effects on metabolic markers have previously been described including on glucose homeostasis, weight reduction (and adipose tissue reduction), lipid profile, and inflammation (hsCRP). Especially, improvement of glucose homeostasis and weight reduction are known pharmacological mechanisms of Ozempic and Wegovy and they constituted the basis of the approval of these medicinal products. In the current dossier, also positive effects on HbA1c were noticed in patients without T2DM for all timepoints of study 4381. Further, clear improvement in weight related parameters were seen for all timepoints evaluated in study 4381. Improvement in lipid profile and inflammation have also already been described. Most notable effects are seen for TG reduction and VLDL, while being less clear for other parameters including LDL-C and HDL-C. For hsCRP, a clear reduction was seen for all timepoints evaluated in study 4381.

Further data on several of these markers and other PD markers have been generated in the dose finding study and the main study which are discussed under efficacy, and generally show improvement in these markers, thereby supporting the primary evaluation.

However, the exact underlying mechanism for the improvement in MASH is not described. For instance, it has been described that GLP-1 is dysregulated in MASH, which triggers multiple pathways including e.g. fibroblast growth factor (FGF)-21 signalling.

Although changes in non-invasive fibrosis biomarkers such as ELF score, Pro-C3, and Fib-4 were reported, these markers do not directly confirm hepatic target engagement. There appears to be an absence of GLP-1 receptor expression on hepatocytes, but the exact mechanism could not be fully elucidated.

Any potential for a pro-arrhythmic effect has previously been studies in the diabetes MAA dossier, and it was concluded that any pro-arrhythmic effect would be unlikely. Extrapolation to the current population was considered reasonable, as there is no substantial deviation to the pro-arrhythmic risk from the existing target populations.

With 3 patients, the number of antibody formation was considered low and no relevant impact on PK was seen.

## 5.2.6.2. Conclusions

Overall, the PK of semaglutide is sufficiently investigated, and the proposed dose is supported from clinical pharmacological point of view. PK in MASH patients is largely comparable to the known PK in patients with T2D or in obese patients, as known from the Ozempic and Wegovy MAA.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

Indirect evidence is generated based on liver associated PD markers and more general (cardio)metabolic markers in the phase 1 study, the dose finding study and the pivotal study, generally supporting the pivotal evaluation in the phase 3 study. The applicant has also elaborated on the possible mechanism of action.

The results of the refinement of exposure-response modelling for fibrosis based on stiffness suggest that liver stiffness does not evolve fully in parallel with fibrosis but may serve as a controlling or intermediate factor in disease progression and could represent a transitional state influencing the probability of progression to the next fibrosis stage. However, at present no firm conclusions can be drawn regarding the clinical relevance of these markers until on-going trials are completed.

Section 4.2 of the SmPC provides a clear distinction between patients with preserved hepatic function (as studied in trial 4553) and those with moderate or severe hepatic impairment, reassuring prescribers regarding the safety of semaglutide in patients with NASH/MASH and preserved hepatic function, while maintaining cautionary statements for patients with more advanced hepatic impairment.

## 5.3. Clinical efficacy

## 5.3.1. Dose response study

The dosing regimen was selected based on an integrated evaluation of efficacy, safety, and exposure considerations from the phase 2 dose-finding Study 4296. The dose was also selected based upon the data from the Ozempic T2D MAA investigating semaglutide SC at doses up to 2.0 mg as well as the Wegovy weight management MAA conducted for semaglutide SC 2.4 mg. See also section 5.2.5.

## Study 4296

- General design

Study 4296 was a 72-week, multinational, multicentre, randomised, double-blind, six-armed, parallelgroup, placebo controlled, multiple-dose trial comparing the efficacy and safety of semaglutide s.c. 0.1 mg, 0.2 mg and 0.4 mg once daily (OD) versus placebo in subjects with MASH. The diagnosis of MASH was confirmed by histological evaluation of liver biopsy samples prior to randomisation.

The total maximum trial duration for the individual subject was 85 weeks, comprising a 6-week screening period, followed by the 72-week randomised treatment period and a follow-up period of 7 weeks. Subjects were randomised in a 3:3:3:1:1:1 ratio to semaglutide s.c. 0.1 mg, 0.2 mg, 0.4 mg OD, or to the corresponding injection volumes of placebo.

After randomisation, the subjects entered a dose-escalation period, with increases in dose every 4 weeks until the target dose was reached. The trial was double-blinded within dose levels but not between dose levels because of the different dose escalation schemes and volumes administered. Subjects who prematurely discontinued trial product were to continue with the originally scheduled site visits/contacts and final assessments (including performing a liver biopsy) until 72 weeks after randomisation. Randomisation was stratified in five groups based on region (Japanese or nonJapanese) and, within the non-Japanese group, based on diabetes status at screening (with or without type 2 diabetes (T2D)) and fibrosis stage of baseline liver biopsy (F1/F2 or F3).

Subjects in all treatment groups, including subjects who prematurely discontinued trial product, received nutritional and physical activity counselling throughout the trial.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

Randomisation (3:1:3:1:3:1)

Semaglutide 0.4 mg OD

Corresponding placebo

- Semaglutide 0.2 mg OD

Corresponding placebo

Figure 6: Trial design - trial 4296

Corresponding placebo

Dose

<!-- image -->

- Included patients

Patients with histologic evidence of MASH based on central pathologist evaluation of a liver biopsy obtained up to 21 weeks before screening; Histological NAFLD activity score (NAS) &gt; 4 with a score of 1 or more in each sub-component of the score based on central pathologist evaluation; MASH fibrosis stage F1, F2 or F3 based on central pathologist evaluation.

- Study objectives

The primary objective was to compare the effect of semaglutide s.c. once daily versus placebo on histological resolution of MASH.

Secondary objectives included investigation of the dose-response relationship of three dose levels of semaglutide s.c. once daily (0.1 mg/day, 0.2 mg/day and 0.4 mg/day) on histological resolution of MASH, effects on liver-related histological parameters and biomarkers of MASH disease, weight related parameters, glucose metabolism, cardiovascular risk factors, patient reported outcomes, and to evaluate the safety and tolerability of three dose levels of semaglutide s.c. once daily in subjects with MASH.

- Study endpoints

The primary endpoint was MASH resolution without worsening of fibrosis after 72 weeks (yes/no)

The most important secondary endpoint was at least one stage of liver fibrosis improvement with no worsening of MASH after 72 weeks. This endpoint was defined as supportive in the protocol and as confirmatory in the Statistical Analysis Plan (SAP) issued before unblinding of the trial.

- Statistical considerations

The SAP was finalised prior to unblinding the trial. The main revisions to the analyses specified in the protocol were:

1. The secondary endpoint fibrosis improvement with no worsening of MASH was added to the confirmatory test procedure and, thus, became a confirmatory secondary endpoint.
2. The population for the confirmatory tests was restricted to only consist of subjects with fibrosis stage 2 or 3 at baseline (that is, excluding subjects with baseline fibrosis stage 1)
- Results

A total of 288 subjects were planned for randomisation; 1227 were screened and 320 were randomised. In total, 302 (94.4%) of the randomised subjects completed the trial and 277 subjects (&gt;86%) had an evaluable biopsy at week 72 (Table 7).

## 5.3.2. Main study

## 5.3.2.1. NN9931-4553

## 5.3.2.1.1. Study title

The effect of semaglutide in subjects with non-cirrhotic non-alcoholic steatohepatitis

## 5.3.2.1.2. Study design

## General design

Trial 4553 is a 240-week randomised, multicentre, double-blind, parallel-group phase 3 trial comparing semaglutide s.c. 2.4 mg once weekly versus placebo s.c. once weekly in subjects with MASH and F2 or F3 (recruitment cap of approximately 20% for subjects with F2). The trial design is outlined in Figure 9.

1.7 mg

Placebo + standard of care

The trial has two parts, with distinctive objectives and endpoints. The objective in part 1 was to demonstrate that semaglutide s.c. 2.4 mg in addition to standard of care improves liver histology after 72 weeks of treatment. The objective in the ongoing part 2 is to demonstrate that semaglutide 2.4 mg in addition to standard of care lowers the risk of liver-related clinical events after 240 weeks of treatment. 4 8 12 16

72

The present application is based on an interim evaluation of part 1.

Figure 9: Trial design - trial 4553

<!-- image -->

N: protocol-specified planned total trial population; s.c. subcutaneous.

## Treatment

The treatment period includes a 16-week dose escalation phase from 0.25 mg to 2.4 mg once weekly, followed by maintenance treatment. Due to the lack of pharmacological treatment options for patients with MASH, placebo was chosen as the comparator on top of standard of care. Two different peninjectors were provided in this trial: the DV3396 single-dose pen-injector (for all countries except China) and the PDS290 pen-injector (for all countries).

## Randomisation

All subjects were screened and centrally randomised using an IWRS (interactive web response system) and assigned to the next available treatment according to randomisation schedule. Eligible subjects were randomised 2:1 to receive either semaglutide or placebo s.c. Randomisation was stratified based on the presence of T2D at screening (yes or no; T2D defined as  a medical history of T2D and/or HbA1c ≥ 48 mmol/mol ( ≥ 6.5%)), fibrosis stage (F2 or F3) and region (Japan, East Asia excluding Japan, or rest of the world). The IWRS is used for blind-breaking.

## Blinding

During the entire trial conduct, trial subjects, investigators, and Novo Nordisk personnel actively involved in the trial conduct were blinded to individual treatment assignments. To preserve the blinding of the trial, a minimum number of Novo Nordisk personnel were able to see the randomisation table and treatment assignments before the trial was complete to conduct the interim analysis. The responsibilities and procedures under which the unblinded team and other personnel would operate to analysis

240

End of treatment

247

End of trial

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

prepare the documentation for regulatory and other filings based on the interim evaluation were described in an interim evaluation charter. As specified in detail in an interim evaluation charter, personnel who had had access to unblinded subject-level data will not be involved in the conduct of the ongoing trial until the end of part 2.

According to standard pharmacovigilance procedures, specific members of the Novo Nordisk A/S Global Safety department were not blinded to SUSARs (for reporting purposes), whereas the clinical trial group and the investigator remained blinded throughout the trial .

Two investigators were designated as signatory investigators for both part 1 and part 2 of the 4553 trial. To avoid unblinding of the signatory investigators towards part 2 of trial 4553, Novo Nordisk as the sponsor of the trial appointed an internal medical specialist as the responsible party for reviewing and approving the interim CTR.

## Histological evaluation

The eligibility biopsies were later re-read along with the week-72 biopsies by one of the pathologist teams to serve as the baseline for the derivation of the histology-related efficacy endpoints. The pathologists were blinded towards subject ID as well as biopsy type (baseline or week 72).

## Patient population

## Before randomisation

All subjects needed either a historical liver biopsy or a trial-related liver biopsy performed before randomisation. To reduce the screen failure rate in subjects undergoing a trial-related biopsy, subjects had to fulfil at least 1 pre-qualification criterion to confirm the likelihood of MASH and significant fibrosis before proceeding to a trial-related liver biopsy.

Pre-qualification criteria were as follows:

- Liver biopsy performed within 180 days prior to V1 which can be sent to central evaluation.
- History of any of the following:
- o ELF ≥9.8
- o FibroScan ® ≥9.1 kPa
- o MRE ≥3.2 kPa
- o FAST ≥0.67
- o Biopsy consistent with MASH and presence of fibrosis stage 2 or 3
- FIB4 ≥1.3 measured at V1.

All subjects underwent a liver biopsy at week 72 (end of part 1), unless they had had a confirmed liver-related clinical event prior to week 72.

## Key inclusion criteria

Subjects were eligible to be included in the trial only if all of the following criteria applied: Informed consent and age above or equal to 18 years at the time of signing informed consent; Histological evidence of MASH based on a central pathologist evaluation of the baseline liver biopsy, of fibrosis stage 2 or stage 3 according to the MASH CRN classification, and NAS ≥ 4 with a score of 1 or more in steatosis, lobular inflammation and hepatocyte ballooning . The baseline liver biopsy can be a historical biopsy obtained within 180 days prior to screening visit (V1).

## Key exclusion criteria

Subjects were excluded from the trial if any of the following relevant criteria applied (in addition to standard exclusion criteria):

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

- Liver related

Documented causes of chronic liver disease other than NAFLD; Positive HBsAg, positive anti-HIV, positive HCV RNA at screening (V2A) or any known presence of HCV RNA or HBsAg within 2 years of screening (V2A); Presence or history of ascites, variceal bleeding, hepatic encephalopathy, spontaneous bacterial peritonitis or liver transplantation at randomisation; ALT or AST &gt; 5 times ULN at screening (V2A), doubling of ALT or AST level between V1 and V2A deemed clinically significant in the opinion of the investigator, total bilirubin &gt; 1.5 mg/dL at screening (V2A) (Total bilirubin level &gt; 1.5 mg/dL is allowed if conjugated bilirubin is within normal range), alkaline phosphatase levels &gt; 2 x ULN at screening (V2A), INR of prothrombin time ≥ 1.35 at screening (V2A); MELD score &gt; 12 points at screening (V2A).

- Concomitant therapy

Known or suspected excessive consumption of alcohol (&gt; 20 g/day for women or &gt; 30 g/day for men) or alcohol dependence (assessed by the Alcohol Use Disorders Identification Test [AUDIT questionnaire]); Treatment with vitamin E (at doses ≥ 800 IU/day) or pioglitazone or medications approved for treatment of MASH which has not been at a stable dose in the period from 90 days prior to the screening visit (V2A). Treatment with GLP-1 RAs in the period from 90 days prior to the screening visit (V2A); Treatment with glucose-lowering agent(s) (other than GLP-1 RAs), lipid-lowering medication or weight loss medication not stable in the opinion of the investigator in the period from 90 days prior to the screening visit (V2A); Previous or planned (during the trial period) obesity treatment with surgery or a weight loss device.

- Carcinoma

Presence or history of hepatocellular carcinoma at randomisation; Presence or history within the past 5 years prior to screening (V2A) of malignant neoplasms other than hepatocellular carcinoma. Basal and squamous cell skin cancer and any carcinoma in-situ are allowed; Personal or first-degree relative(s) history of multiple endocrine neoplasia type 2 or medullary thyroid carcinoma.

- Diabetes related

HbA1c &gt; 80 mmol/mol (9.5%) at screening (V2A); For subjects with T2D: Uncontrolled and potentially unstable diabetic retinopathy or maculopathy. History or presence of type 1 diabetes.

- CV related

Any of the following: myocardial infarction, stroke, classification of heart failure New York Heart Association (NYHA) Class IV, hospitalisation for unstable angina pectoris or transient ischaemic attack within the past 90 days prior to the day of screening (V2A) and between screening and randomisation.

- Body weight related

Unstable body weight defined as more than 5% self-reported change in body weight in the period from 90 days prior to the screening visit (V1); Any subject where a substantial weight loss in the investigator's opinion might jeopardise subject's safety.

- Others

eGFR &lt; 30 mL/min/1.73 m 2  ( CKDEPI) at screening (V2A); Thrombocyte count less than 150,000 per microliter of blood (V2A), unless it reflects the patient's habitual thrombocytes level and there is no presence of portal hypertension in the opinion of the investigator; Presence of acute pancreatitis within the past 180 days prior to screening (V2A); History or presence of chronic pancreatitis at screening (V2A); Any condition which, in the investigator's opinion might jeopardise subject's safety or compliance with the protocol.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

## Countries

The trial is expected to be conducted in 39 countries. For Part 1 (in scope for the current interim CTR), 334 sites enrolled 1197 subjects in 38 countries.

## 5.3.2.1.3. Objectives and estimands

## Primary objective

The primary objective for Part 1 is to demonstrate that treatment with semaglutide s.c. 2.4 mg improves liver histology compared to placebo in subjects with MASH and fibrosis stage 2 or 3.

## Statistical methods for estimation and sensitivity analysis on primary estimands

## General considerations

The 'in-trial' observation period was defined as the time period where the subject was to be be followed in the trial. The in-trial observation period for a subject begins on the date of randomisation and ends at the first of the following dates (both inclusive):

- follow-up visit (end-of-trial visit);
- withdrawal of consent;
- last contact with subject (for subjects lost to follow-up);
- death.

In the case that the part 1 interim was based on the first 800 randomised trial subjects, the primary data cut for efficacy evaluations was based on the analysis set 'FAS (interim)' and the in-trial observation period truncated at week 72. Otherwise, the primary data cut was based on the analysis set 'FAS (interim, all randomised subjects)' and the in-trial observation period truncated at week 72. In either case, supplementary efficacy evaluations were made based on the analysis set 'FAS (interim, all randomised subjects)' and the in-trial observation period. For both FAS (interim) and FAS (interim, all randomised subjects), subjects were analysed according to the trial product they were randomised to receive.

The safety evaluations were based on the analysis set 'SAS (interim)' and on-treatment observation period as the primary safety data cut. All subjects assigned to trial treatment and who take at least 1 dose of trial product. Subjects were analysed according to the trial product they received. The in-trial observation period was used for selected safety areas.

For confirmatory endpoints controlled for multiplicity, one-sided p-values were used for test of superiority. For reporting of results, the estimated treatment effect and the 95% confidence interval was accompanied by the two-sided p-value.

Vital status, non-fatal events and other assessments were not systematically collected after withdrawal of consent, lost-to-follow-up, or after end-of-trial. For this reason, any event occurring, or data ascertained after the in-trial observation period that were reported in the trial database, were be included in analyses, unless otherwise stated.

If not otherwise specified, the baseline measurement was defined as the latest available eligible measurement at or prior to the randomisation visit. For the analysis of continuous statistical data, subjects with no available baseline assessment were omitted.

Baseline diabetes status and baseline fibrosis stage were used as stratification variables for the randomisation and were consequently included as factors in the statistical analyses. The diabetes status factor was based on medical history. The fibrosis stage factor was defined by the histological evaluation that was used for determining eligibility. The randomisation was also stratified based on region; however, including all three stratification variables in the analyses may create too small strata and lead to unreliable estimates. The factors in the analyses have therefore been restricted to the two that were considered most relevant.

For deriving the histological endpoints, the baseline biopsies were re-read twice; one time for the week 72 analysis and one time for the week 240 analysis. The endpoints were derived by comparing the

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

reading of the in-trial biopsy (week 72/week 240) with the corresponding re-read baseline biopsy. For the analyses of liver histology at week 72 and week 240, only biopsies with ascertainment dates within a visit window of minus/plus 180 days around 72 weeks and 240 weeks from baseline, respectively, were included.

For adjudicated events, the onset date was defined based on the outcome of the evaluation of the event as defined by the EAC.

Co-participation in COVID-19 trials was handled like an intercurrent event. In line with the prespecified treatment policy estimand including the handling of intercurrent events, co-participation were regarded as an integrated part of the treatment regimen.

## Primary estimands

The two primary endpoints, 'Resolution of steatohepatitis and no worsening in liver fibrosis at 72 weeks', and 'Improvement in liver fibrosis and no worsening in steatohepatitis at 72 weeks', as well as the confirmatory secondary endpoint 'Resolution of steatohepatitis and improvement in liver fibrosis' were analysed using a Cochran-Mantel-Haenszel (CMH) test stratified into four strata defined by baseline diabetes status (with or without T2D) and baseline fibrosis stage (F2 or F3). Missing data was handled by reference-based imputation, i.e. a method of multiple imputation that is informed by data from subjects in the placebo group. It is thereby assumed that subjects in either treatment group without an observed outcome have the same chances of meeting the endpoint as subjects in the placebo group with an observed outcome. Subjects who have had a liver-related clinical event before the week 72 visit will be handled as non-responders, i.e. these subjects were not considered to have met the endpoint.

Inference on the below statistical procedure for the confirmatory endpoints was adjusted for the group sequential design. Adjustment of estimates, p-values and two-sided 95% confidence intervals were calculated using stage-wise ordering. The adjustment procedure used the available information defined as the reciprocal of the squared standard error from the corresponding analysis as specified below. Note that analysis results were estimated and reported at either intermediate stage or final stage for all part 1 endpoints regardless of at which stage superiority was established as per the look-back approach by Örhn.

A two-way tipping point sensitivity analysis was conducted to investigate the impact of the assumptions underlying the imputation procedure on the conclusion. Testing of superiority followed the same approach as the main analytical approach; however, instead of sampling missing data from the observed data in the placebo group, a prespecified range of responder proportions was used to draw responses in each arm from which multiple complete data sets are generated. The grid used for responder rates in each arm was given by 1% to 99% by increments of 1%-point.

The handling of missing data in the main analytical approach was aligned with a treatment policy strategy. As a sensitivity analysis, all subjects with missing data were alternatively classified as nonresponders according to a composite strategy. The analysis was performed as a CMH test stratified by baseline fibrosis stage and baseline diabetes status in the same way as in the main analytical approach using a single imputation only.

The main analyses of binary liver histology endpoints were supplemented by evaluating the relative risk as treatment effect estimate. Specifically, the Mantel-Haenszel estimate of the common relative risk in responder proportions was used with the corresponding Greenland and Robins estimate of the variance. Estimates and standard errors from multiple imputed data were pooled using Rubin's rule analogous with main approach.

To complement the interpretation of liver histology, a different clinical question was targeted in a

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

supplementary analysis. In this analysis, a hypothetical strategy was used to handle the two intercurrent events of (i) premature trial product discontinuations and (ii) initiation of ancillary therapy, possibly modifying MASH. The analysis of the primary part 1 endpoints was targeting a treatment effect that quantifies the effect of taking the randomised trial product as planned.

Operationally, this means that data collected after the occurrence of an intercurrent event of these types are set to missing and instead imputed according to the hypothetical strategy. The statistical analysis was identical to the primary part 1 analysis, with the exception that missing data was imputed differently. In line with the hypothetical strategy, missing data, regardless of the reason, was handled by multiple imputation informed by observed data from study subjects within their own treatment arm.

## Secondary objectives

The secondary objectives are as follows:

- To demonstrate that treatment with semaglutide s.c. 2.4 mg lowers body weight compared to placebo in subjects with NASH and fibrosis stages 2 or 3.
- To demonstrate that treatment with semaglutide s.c. 2.4 mg improves patient-reported outcomes compared to placebo in subjects with MASH and fibrosis stages 2 or 3.
- To compare the effects of semaglutide s.c. 2.4 mg versus placebo on cardiovascular disease and cardio-metabolic factors in subjects with MASH and fibrosis stages 2 or 3.
- To compare the effect of semaglutide s.c. 2.4 mg versus placebo on biomarkers related to fibrosis in subjects with MASH and fibrosis stages 2 or 3.

## Estimand for the secondary objectives

Endpoints for secondary objectives were split between confirmatory and supportive. Estimands were only described for the former.

The population and treatment attributes of the estimand for secondary objectives were the same as for primary objectives.

The endpoint (and respective population-level summary) for confirmatory secondary objectives was defined as follows:

- Change in body weight from randomisation (week 0) to week 72 (absolute difference in percentage between treatments);
- Resolution of steatohepatitis and improvement in liver fibrosis from randomisation (week 0) to week 72  a, b  (absolute difference in proportion between treatments in achieving both);
- o a. Resolution of steatohepatitis is defined as an NAS of 0-1 for inflammation, 0 for ballooning, and any value for steatosis (according to NASH CRN2);
- o b. Improvement in fibrosis is defined as ≥ 1 grade improvement on the NASH CRN fibrosis scale;
- Change in SF-36 bodily pain from randomisation (week 0) to week 72 (absolute difference in score points between treatments);
- Change in body weight from randomisation (week 0) to week 240 (absolute difference in percentage between treatments)

Intercurrent currents of premature treatment discontinuation and initiation of ancillary therapy possibly modifying MASH were handled for confirmatory secondary objectives as for primary objectives, with a treatment policy strategy.

The intercurrent events of liver-induced MELD score ≥ 15, hepatic decompensation events, unplanned

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

biopsy showing progression to cirrhosis, liver transplantation and death were handled for confirmatory secondary objectives as for primary objectives for resolution of steatohepatitis and improvement in liver fibrosis because it is a binary variable: each event will be counted as a negative response.

For other endpoints (change in body weight and change in SF-36 bodily pain), the estimand strategy was not provided for these five intercurrent events.

Supportive secondary endpoints are listed in the following table.

## Statistical methods for estimation and sensitivity analysis on the secondary estimands

Change in body weight from baseline to at 72 weeks and change in SF-36 bodily pain from baseline to 72 weeks was analysed using an analysis of covariance model (ANCOVA) with missing data and data non-ascertainable due to death handled by unconditional reference-based imputation, i.e. imputation of missing data was to be informed by data from subjects in the placebo group.

Subjects with any missing baseline assessment used in the model available were omitted. The analysis was performed in the following steps:

1. An ANCOVA model with baseline diabetes status, baseline fibrosis stage and diabetes-byfibrosis interaction as factors, and baseline body weight (kg) as a covariate was fitted to the placebo data week 72 only.
2. Five hundred sets of estimates of the model parameters are drawn from the posterior distribution. For each replicated set of parameter estimates, the model was used to generate a

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

- complete dataset by imputing missing data based on subjects' baseline diabetes status, baseline fibrosis stage and baseline body weight.
3. Each complete data set was analysed separately using an ANCOVA model with treatment, baseline diabetes status, baseline fibrosis stage and diabetes-by-fibrosis interaction as factors, and baseline body weight as a covariate. The estimated treatment differences and standard errors were then pooled using Rubin's rule to draw inference.

Resolution of steatohepatitis and improvement in liver fibrosis at 72 weeks were analysed using the same CMH test and multiple imputation of missing data as for the primary binary endpoints. Like the primary binary endpoints, both the absolute treatment difference and the relative treatment difference were estimated.

For the continuous confirmatory secondary endpoints, a tipping point analysis was carried out. A penalty term was added to the imputed values from the main analysis at week 72. The approach was to explore a range of penalties for both treatment groups, and the impact these would have on the trial conclusions. This sensitivity analysis evaluated the robustness of the superiority conclusions to departures from the observed change in the continuous confirmatory secondary endpoint in both treatment groups. The grid of the penalty terms for the tipping point analysis of body weight is -30 to 30 by increments of 1, the penalty term was to be added as %-points. For SF-36 bodily pain the grid for the tipping point analysis is -15 to 15 by increments of 1, the penalty term was added as absolute change.

For the binary confirmatory secondary endpoint, if superiority was confirmed, a two-way tipping point analysis similar to the analysis for the primary endpoints was carried out.

Secondary binary endpoints were analysed using the same CMH test and handling of missing data as for the primary binary endpoints. Secondary continuous endpoints were analysed using the same type of ANCOVA with multiple imputation as for change in body weight.

## Tertiary objectives

The tertiary objectives are as follows:

- To compare the effect of semaglutide s.c. 2.4 mg versus placebo on insulin sensitivity in subjects with MASH and fibrosis stages 2 or 3.
- To compare the effect of semaglutide s.c. 2.4 mg versus placebo on cognitive function in subjects with MASH and fibrosis stages 2 or 3.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

- b) Assessed by Stroop Color and Word Test and The Portosystemic Encephalopathy Syndrome Test (PSE Syndrome Test)

Abbreviations: HOMA-IR = homeostatic model assessment - insulin resistance, TNF-alpha = Tumour necrosis factor alpha, Il-1 RA = Interleukin-1 receptor agonist protein

Estimands are not defined for these endpoints.

## Statistical methods for estimation and sensitivity analysis on the tertiary estimands

Exploratory continuous endpoints were analysed using the same type of ANCOVA with multiple imputation as for change in body weight. The model included treatment, baseline diabetes status, baseline fibrosis stage and diabetes-by-fibrosis interaction as factors, baseline body weight and baseline value of the endpoint as covariates. Continuous endpoints which were analysed as a ratio to baseline would be logarithmically transformed for the statistical analysis, together with their baseline value. The estimated treatment differences were back-transformed to the original scale and expressed as treatment ratios.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

## Deviations from study plan

In total, 1163 important protocol deviations were reported before interim DBL (7 were trial-level deviations, no country-level deviations, 137 were site-level deviations, 1019 were subject-level deviations) but were not likely to have affected the statistical inferences, analyses, subject safety, or conclusions presented in this report.

After randomisation 0 vs 18 (6.8%) started semaglutide treatment.

Patients with a BMI &lt; 25 kg/m 2  and BMI 25-30 kg/m 2  were older (mean 65 (10.3) and 59 (10.4)), more women (77% and 59%) and more Asian (77% and 45%) vs White, and less from Europe (9.4% and 23%) compared to the entire study population. Other baseline characteristics were more or less comparable for these subgroups compared to the entire study population.

| Sema 2.4 mg Placebo             | 16.54 [10.24; 22.83]                    | <0.0001                         | 0.0045                          | 32.7% (175 of 234 subjects) 16.1% (43 of 266 subjects) | Superiority confirmed           |

| Sema 2.4 mg Placebo             | 1.34 [0.01; 2.67]                       | 0.0244                          | 0.0045                          | -                                                      | Superiority not confirmed       |

A tipping point sensitivity analysis was performed evaluating the robustness of the assumptions about the missing data. Additionally, a sensitivity analysis was performed where all subjects with missing data were alternatively classified as non-responders. The tipping point analysis and sensitivity analyses for the primary endpoints supported the conclusions for the main analytical approach.

The hierarchical testing was violated at the Change in SF-36 bodily Pain endpoint.

At week 72, the estimated mean change from week baseline in body weight was -10.51 kg vs -2.04 kg. At week 72, the estimated change from week baseline in SF-36 Bodily pain was 0.86 vs -0.47.

## 5.3.5. Supportive studies

## Study 4492

This was a 48-week randomised, double-blind, placebo-controlled, parallel group, multi-centre trial in 72 patients with histologic evidence of MASH and fibrosis stage 4, a histological NAFLD activity score (NAS) ≥ 3, and a Body mass index ≥27 kg/m 2 . There was a 6-weeks screening period followed by a randomisation visit and a 48-week treatment period. The treatment period was divided into a dose escalation period of 16 weeks and a maintenance period of 32 weeks. The follow-up period was 7 weeks. Total trial duration was approximately 61 weeks. Subjects were randomised in a 2:1 ratio to receive either: semaglutide s.c. 2.4 mg once-weekly or semaglutide placebo s.c. once-weekly as an adjunct to a reduced-calorie diet and increased physical activity. To mitigate the risk of GI side effects, the trial included an initial 16-week dose-escalation period during which the dose was gradually increased to the maintenance dose of 2.4 mg once weekly.

## Trial EX9536-4388

Trial EX9536-4388 was a randomised and placebo-controlled phase 3b CVOT that in a confirmatory analysis demonstrated that Wegovy reduces the risk of major adverse cardiovascular events (MACE; a composite outcome comprising cardiovascular death, non-fatal myocardial infarction, or non-fatal stroke) in adults with established cardiovascular disease and either obesity or overweight. Results from SELECT have been assessed by CHMP under Wegovy and are reflected in the current SmPC for Wegovy.

The effect of semaglutide 2.4 mg on the risk of MACE was assessed based on analyses of clinically relevant subpopulations of subjects at risk of MASH in trial EX9536-4388, identified using baseline levels of the FIB-4 biomarker.

From the full population of 17,604 subjects in SELECT, a subpopulation of 6,442 subjects was identified as being at risk of MASH based on the FIB-4 threshold of 1.3, exclusion of other causes of chronic liver disease as pre-specified in the SAP. Hereafter, this subpopulation will be referred to as 'subjects at risk of MASH' and will be the primary subpopulation in the evaluation of efficacy. This cut-off of FIB-4 ≥ 1.3 regardless of age was pre-specified prior to the database lock of trials ESSENCE and SELECT and followed the European guidelines available at the time of trial execution.

Additionally, two supplemental analyses were conducted to assess consistency. The subpopulation at \"age-adjusted risk of MASH\" was defined to consider recent clinical evidence and new guidelines from 2024. Lastly, a subpopulation of subjects \"at increased risk of MASH\" was defined based on a high FIB4 cut-off.

In the pre-specified subpopulation of subjects from EX9536-4388 at risk of MASH, semaglutide 2.4 mg was associated with a reduction in the risk of first event adjudication committee (EAC)-confirmed MACE of 26%, HR: 0.74 [0.63; 0.88]95% CI, thus confirming superiority. This was supported by the analysis in the subpopulation of subjects at age-adjusted risk of MASH, were the risk reduction of first EAC-confirmed MACE was 21% with a 95% CI. In the subpopulation of subjects at increased risk of MASH, the risk reduction of first EAC-confirmed MACE was 36%.

Regarding the individual subcomponents of the composite MACE-endpoint, the outcomes of CV death and non-fatal myocardial infarction (MI) attained similar benefits across the EX9536-4388 subpopulations at risk of MASH, age-adjusted and at increased risk of MASH, respectively. Similar to estimate

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

the EX9536-4388 main population, the overall reduction in MACE was mainly driven by a reduction in CV-death and non-fatal MI. Like the total EX9536-4388 population, the all-cause death was equally reduced through all three subpopulations. Overall, the tendency was clear, the risk of MACE was lower in individuals at risk of MASH as well as age-adjusted risk of MASH when treated with semaglutide 2.4 mg.

In summary, the prespecified analysis of the subpopulation at risk of MASH showed a reduction in the risk of first EAC-confirmed MACE events. This was further supported by the additional analysis of the age-adjusted subpopulation and the subpopulation with an increased risk of MASH.
