N: number of subjects with at least one event; %: percentage of subjects with at least one event; E: number of events; R: events per 100 years of exposure; AE: adverse event; SAE: serious adverse event; PT: preferred term; SAS (interim): safety analysis set - all exposed subjects.

Values for PYI (patient-years at risk of incident event) and I (incidence [number of incident events per 100 PYI]) are available in the CSR.

Hypoglycaemic episodes are categorised according to the ADA 2023 classification.

Based on the ADA 2023 classification, the majority of episodes were level 1. Hypoglycaemic episodes of level 2 or 3 (combined) were reported by 7.4% of the subjects with semaglutide 2.4 mg and 5.4% with placebo. Of the subjects experiencing level 2 or 3 hypoglycaemic episodes, approximately half in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group and 2/3 in the placebo group were treated with sulfonylureas and/or insulin at the time of the episode. For most of the subjects potential contributing factors (e.g., treatment with insulin or sulfonylureas or changes in diet or missed meals) were present. Two (2) subjects needed IV glucose treatment. For half of the events, plasma-glucose values were &gt;3.0 mmol/L (54 mg/dL).

Episodes of severe hypoglycaemia (level 3) were reported by 2.2% (corresponding to 10 subjects) in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group and 0.5% (corresponding to 1 subject) in the placebo group, requiring IV treatment in 2 out of  11 subjects . The frequency of severe hypoglycaemia on semaglutide 2.4 mg in study 4553 is higher than previously seen with for subjects with T2D in semaglutide 2.4 mg for weight management, with only 1 severe event of hypoglycaemia reported (0.2% of subjects, 0.002 events/patient year).

Hypoglycaemia based on AEs . Of subjects without T2D , 1 subject in each treatment group reported 1 event of hypoglycaemia. Both events were non-serious and mild in severity, resolved and did not lead to premature trial product discontinuation.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

Onset of T2D during trial 4553 . A total of 11 subjects in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group and 18 subjects in the placebo group had onset of T2D during trial 4553.

## Supportive studies

Study 4296 The subpopulation of subjects with T2D comprised 62.2% of the total population. A higher proportion of subjects with T2D reported hypoglycaemic episodes in the semaglutide 0.1 mg and 0.4 mg groups than in the placebo group, with no dose-relationship across the semaglutide groups. In the semaglutide groups, 5.9% to 10.2% of the subjects reported severe or BG confirmed symptomatic episodes (Novo Nordisk classification) versus 4.0% in the placebo group.

Based on the ADA 2013 classification, documented symptomatic episodes accounted for the majority of the hypoglycaemic episodes, and occurred in the highest proportion of subjects and at the highest rates in the semaglutide 0.4 mg group. A total of 4 severe episodes (none in the semaglutide 0.4 mg group) and 15 probable symptomatic episodes were reported in the semaglutide groups.

## 5.4.4.9. Retinal disorders

Diabetic retinopathy is a common microvascular complication of diabetes. Rapid improvements in glycaemic control may be associated with an initial worsening of diabetic retinopathy. This risk has especially been seen in patients with long-standing diabetes, poor glycaemic control, rapid or large drop in HbA1c and pre-existing diabetic retinopathy. Complications of diabetic retinopathy is a known risk with treatment of semaglutide for patients with T2D, hence subjects with uncontrolled and potentially unstable diabetic retinopathy or maculopathy were excluded from all the trials. In trial 4553, the risk of retinal disorders is evaluated based on a pre-defined MedDRA search, data from event specific form, and eye-examinations that were performed during trial conduct for subjects with T2D. The evaluation is based on the in-trial observation period, due to the potential long latency between onset and diagnosis.

## Study 4553

In trial 4553 (interim evaluation), for subjects with T2D at baseline AEs of retinal disorders were reported by comparable proportions of subjects and event rates with semaglutide 2.4 mg (3.1%, 2.6 events per 100 PYO) compared to placebo (4.1%, 3.2 events per 100 PYO). One (1) event in each group was moderate in severity, the rest of the reported AEs were mild. The majority of events were not resolved. All AEs were considered unlikely related to trial product, and none led to treatment discontinuation. The most frequently reported PT was diabetic retinopathy with 5 subjects in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group reporting a total of 7 events and 3 subjects in the placebo group reporting a total of 3 events. The remaining AEs were distributed across multiple PTs with few events reported for each. One (1) SAE of age-related macular degeneration was reported. The event was moderate in severity and not resolved. It was considered unlikely related to trial product and did not lead to premature trial product discontinuation.

A total of 12 AEs reported by 10 subjects and deemed due to a new onset or worsening of diabetic retinopathy had additional data collection. All events were non-serious, mild and non-proliferative, with no worsening in visual acuity and did not require treatment.

## Supportive studies

Study 4296 The number of subjects reporting events of retinal disorders was 1 (1.3%), 3 (3.8%) and 1 (1.2%) on semaglutide 0.1 mg, 0.2 mg, and 0.4 mg, respectively, and was 1.3% in the placebo group. No serious adverse events were reported. Two (2) subjects in the semaglutide 0.2 mg group had 1 event each of diabetic retinopathy, the rest of the events comprised retinal artery occlusion,

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

visual impairment and vitreous floaters. All of the events were non-serious, none led to treatment discontinuation, and all were mild in severity. All of the events were judged by the investigator as unlikely related to trial product.

## 5.4.4.10. Immunogenicity

The risk of immunogenicity is evaluated based on assessments of anti-semaglutide antibodies at selected site visits. Furthermore, immunogenicity is evaluated by two pre-defined MedDRA searches, allergic reactions and injection site reactions based on the on-treatment observation period.

## Antibodies

An overview of the ADA incidences and impact on efficacy and safety for the marketed GLP-1 RA products based on human GLP-1 are presented in Table 42. All marketed analogues of human GLP-1 have similar and low incidences of ADA development (below 10%). The ADAs are of a low titre, few are cross-reactive to endogenous GLP-1 and even fewer are in vitro neutralising.

Table 42: Marketed GLP-1 receptor agonists - observed immunogenicity and impact on efficacy and safety

| GLP-1 receptor agonist              | Wegovy ®          | Ozempic ®         | Rybelsus ®        | Victoza ®         | Saxenda ®                     | Trulicity ®       |
|-------------------------------------|-------------------|-------------------|-------------------|-------------------|-------------------------------|-------------------|
| Administration                      | s.c.              | s.c.              | Oral              | s.c.              | s.c.                          | s.c.              |
| Active drug                         | Semaglutide       | Semaglutide       | Semaglutide       | Liraglutide       | Liraglutide                   | Dulaglutide       |
| Indication                          | weight management | T2D               | T2D               | T2D               | weight management             | T2D               |
| Homology to human GLP-1             | 94%               | 94%               | 94%               | 97%               | 97%                           | 90%               |
| Level of ADA in Phase 3             | 3% (low titres)   | 1-2% (low titres) | 0.5% (low titres) | 8.6% (low titres) | 2.8% (low titres)             | 1.6% (low titres) |
| Level of cross- reactivity to GLP-1 | 2.0%              | 0.6%              | 0.2%              | 4.8-6.9%          | -                             | 0.9%              |
| Level of in vitro neutralising ADA  | 0%                | 0%                | 0%                | 1.0-2.3%          | 1.2%                          | 0.9%              |
| Impact on efficacy                  | None              | None              | None              | None              | None                          | None              |
| Impact on safety                    | None              | None              | None              | None              | Mild injection site reactions | -                 |

ADA: anti-drug antibodies; GLP-1: glucagon-like peptide 1; %: Percentage of the treated patients with antibody measurements; - : information not available. Based on human-GLP-1

## Study 4553

A total of 763 subjects were exposed to semaglutide 2.4 mg and of these, a total of 3 subjects (0.4%) were positive for anti-semaglutide antibodies. None of these subjects were positive for antibodies at

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

baseline. All 3 subjects with anti-semaglutide antibodies, had antibodies that cross-reacted with native GLP-1. The levels of the antibody response ranged between titre 15-120 with a median titre of 30. One of the three subjects was only positive at one timepoint. However, no further visits had been collected and analysed after this timepoint for this subject. The other two subjects were positive at multiple visits at least 16 weeks apart. Anti-semaglutide antibodies first occurred in week 24. The on-going status of trial 4553 means that neutralising effect of the antibodies was not assessed, as this assessment requires wash out of semaglutide.

AE reported by subjects with ADA was evaluated and compared to the total AE reporting. The events reported by the 3 subjects were mainly GI AEs and mostly PT Nausea. The majority of these AEs were non-serious; the events were mild to moderate in severity and mostly resolved and did not lead to change in dose. No SAEs were reported. One AE of allergic reaction (Eczema) was reported by 1 subject, and no AEs of injections site reactions were reported by 3 subjects positive for antisemaglutide antibodies by the data cut-off date. Based on the reported AEs in subjects with antisemaglutide antibodies at any time and compared to subjects without anti-semaglutide antibodies, there was no indication that formation of anti-semaglutide antibodies impacted the safety profile of semaglutide 2.4 mg.

Allergic reactions . AEs of allergic reactions were reported by comparable proportions of subjects and event rates for semaglutide 2.4 mg (7.6%, 5.2 events per 100 PYE) compared to placebo (6.8%, 5.6 events per 100 PYE) for the on-treatment period. All but one of the AEs were non-serious, and of these, all but one were mild or moderate in severity. The majority of the AEs were judged by the investigator to be unlikely related to trial product. None of the AEs led to premature trial product discontinuation. The most frequently reported PTs were rash (in 2.1% of subjects on semaglutide versus 1.3% of subjects on placebo), eczema (1.6% vs 1.5%), and urticaria (0.5% vs 1.0%). One (1) SAE related to allergic reactions was reported as the PT Allergy to vaccine, deemed unrelated to trial product by the investigator.

Injection site reactions . AEs of injection-site reactions were reported by comparable proportions of subjects and the same event rates for semaglutide 2.4 mg (4.0%, 3.2 events per 100 PYE) compared to placebo (4.3%, 3.2 events per 100 PYE) for the on-treatment period. All AEs were non-serious, and mild or moderate in severity. More than half of the events were assessed as probably or possibly related to trial product by the investigator. None of the AEs led to premature trial product discontinuation. The most frequently reported PTs were Injection site bruising (1.8% vs 2.0 %) and injection site pain (1.1% vs 1.0%).

## Supportive studies

Study 4296 . A total of 239 subjects were exposed to semaglutide OD, and 7 subjects distributed across the semaglutide treatment groups tested positive for anti-semaglutide antibodies, which were mostly of a transient nature; none of the subjects tested positive at baseline. The levels of antisemaglutide antibodies in terms of % B/T were low (&lt;7.0% bound antibodies in the radioimmunoassay).

A total of 6 subjects (corresponding to 86% of all ADA positive subjects in trial 4296) had antibodies that cross-reacted with native GLP-1. No subjects had anti-semaglutide antibodies with in vitro neutralising effect against semaglutide or endogenous GLP-1. None of the anti-semaglutide antibody positive subjects had any antibodies with clinically relevant effect.

The proportion of subjects reporting allergic reactions ranged between 6.2% and 9.0% in the semaglutide groups versus 7.5% in the placebo group, most frequently rash with only a few of moderate severity. The proportion of subjects reporting injection-site reactions ranged between 5.0%

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

7.7% in the semaglutide groups versus 6.3% in the placebo group, most frequently injection site bruising.

## 5.4.4.11. Psychiatric disorders

GLP-1 receptors are expressed in multiple tissues and organs in the body, including the brain. For centrally acting drugs, there is attention to the potential to affect mental health. No associations between semaglutide and mood-related disorders, or suicidal ideation or behaviour have been described in the clinical development programmes for semaglutide for T2D or weight management.

## Study 4553

AEs of psychiatric disorders were reported by comparable proportions of subjects and event rates with semaglutide 2.4 mg (8.6%, 6.8 events per 100 PYE) and placebo (7.3%, 5.4 events per 100 PYE). The majority of the events were mild. One (1) event in each treatment group were considered severe. In both treatment groups, approximately 2/3 of the events were not resolved. The remaining events were either considered resolved or resolving. In both treatment groups, the most frequently reported PTs were insomnia, depression and anxiety. The remaining AEs were distributed across multiple PTs with few events for each PT. The majority of AEs were considered unlikely related to trial product. One (1) event in each treatment group led to treatment discontinuation. A total of 2 SAEs were reported (PTs suicide attempt and depression).

PT insomnia was reported by a higher proportion of subjects and higher rates in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group compared to placebo (4.1% versus 1.8%). All events were non-serious, mild or moderate in severity and most were considered unlikely related to trial product. No pattern relating to the onset of events was observed. No imbalance was observed in the development programme with semaglutide 2.4 mg for weight management (Wegovy).

## Supportive studies

Study 4296 The proportion of subjects reporting psychiatric disorders ranged between 6.3%-9.9% in the semaglutide groups and was 10.0% in the placebo group. The most frequently reported PT was insomnia. The remaining AEs were distributed across multiple PTs with few events for each PT. SAEs of psychiatric disorders were reported by 2 subjects in the semaglutide 0.1 mg group. One (1) subject had 1 severe event of worsening of bipolar disorder leading to discontinuation of trial product and 1 subject had 1 event of major depression and 2 events of suicidal ideation. The relationship to trial product was considered possible in one case of suicidal ideation and unlikely for the other 3 SAEs.

## 5.4.4.12. Medication errors, drug abuse or misuse, and overdose

In study 4553, AEs of medication errors (classified as wrong dose or wrong frequency) were reported by comparable proportions of subjects and event rates with semaglutide 2.4 mg (1.6%, 1.0 events per 100 PYE) compared to placebo (0.8%, 0.5 events per 100 PYE). Four (4) of the events were related to technical complaints. In study 4296, medication errors were reported by similar proportions of subjects with semaglutide and placebo (1.3%-3.7% versus 2.5%). In total, 6 events occurred on trial day 1, where the subjects were administered a higher dose of trial product than intended according to the dose escalation schedule.

In study 4553, events of overdose were reported in 0.8% of subjects on semaglutide compared to 0.5% of subjects on placebo. All events were non-serious, mild, resolved and did not lead to trial product discontinuation.  1 suicide attempt was reported as an SAE. The event was serious and severe

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

and trial product was discontinued. The event resolved and was considered unrelated to the trial product.

## 5.4.4.13. COVID-19

In trial 4553 (interim evaluation), AEs of COVID-19 were reported by comparable proportions of subjects and event rates with semaglutide 2.4 mg (17.9%, 11.8 events per 100 PYE) compared to placebo (21.5%, 14.8 events per 100 PYE). The majority of events were non-serious, mild, resolved and considered unlikely related to trial product. SAEs were reported by 3 subjects in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group and 2 subjects in the placebo group. Trial 4296 was minimally impacted by COVID-19, since the pandemic emerged as the last subjects were to attend their last visit.

## 5.4.4.14. Deaths

The evaluation of deaths is based on investigator-reported AEs with fatal outcome in pivotal study 4553 as well as in all 3 supportive trials. For trials 4553 and 4296, the evaluations are also based on event adjudication by the external adjudication committee (EAC).

## Study 4553

In total, 9 deaths occurred among subjects exposed to trial products. Of these, 3 subjects (3 events) were in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group, and 6 subjects (9 events) were in the placebo group (semaglutide 2.4 mg: 0.4% of subjects, 0.2 events per 100 PYO; placebo: 1.5% of subjects, 1.3 events per 100 PYO).

No clustering of causes was seen, and all fatal events were assessed as unlikely related to trial product by the investigator and by Novo Nordisk.

## Supportive studies

In study 4296 one (1) death occurred in a subject exposed to semaglutide 0.2 mg once daily. This event was assessed as unlikely related to trial product. No deaths were reported in the supportive trials 4492 and 4381.

## 5.4.4.15. Other serious adverse events

## Study 4553

SAEs were reported by comparable proportions of subjects and event rates with semaglutide 2.4 mg (13.0%, 11.9 events per 100 PYE) compared to placebo (12.9%, 12.3 events per 100 PYE) (Table 35). Less than half of the events were severe with both semaglutide 2.4 mg and placebo. In both groups, the majority of the subjects were reported to have recovered from their SAEs. Fatal outcomes were reported for a total of 9 subjects; 3 subjects in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group and 6 subjects in the placebo group ( see section Deaths ). The majority of events were assessed as unlikely related to trial product and did not lead to premature trial product discontinuation.

The most frequently reported SAEs were within the SOCs Infections and infestations and Gastrointestinal disorders. SAEs related to gastrointestinal disorders were reported for a higher proportion of subjects with semaglutide 2.4 mg compared to placebo (in 20 subjects (2.5%) vs. 6 subjects (1.5%)). Infections/inflammation seen in more than one case were appendicitis (n= 3), diverticulitis (n= 3), and Covid-19 (n= 6). Gastro-intestinal disorders seen more than once were intestinal polyps (n= 5), ischaemic colitis (n= 2), gastritis (n= 2), nausea (n= 2), and pancreatitis (n=

## Supportive studies

Study 4296 SAEs were reported by 14.8%-19.2% in the semaglutide groups and 10% in the placebo group. There was no apparent dose-relationship among the semaglutide groups with regards to the proportion of subjects with serious or severe AEs, or with AEs with outcome not resolved.

Across treatment groups, the SAEs were distributed across multiple SOCs. The most frequently reported SAEs across all treatment groups belonged to the SOCs Infections and infestations (10 events in 7 subjects) and Gastrointestinal disorders (8 events in 8 subjects; all in the semaglutide groups), single events of constipation, ischaemic colitis, intestinal diverticulum, megacolon, abdominal adhesion, colitis, gastro-intestinal polyps, and haemorrhage. The majority of the SAEs reported in the trial were reported as single events and judged by the investigator as unlikely related to trial product. In all treatment groups, SAEs were reported throughout the trial. Five (5) SAEs lead to premature trial product discontinuation; all were single PTs from different SOCs with nothing in common.

Study 4492 SAEs were reported by 12.8% of the subjects in the semaglutide group compared to 8.3% in the placebo group. The SAEs were distributed across multiple SOCs. The most frequently reported SAEs in both treatment groups belonged to the SOC Gastrointestinal disorders. Apart from this no clustering of events was observed and the majority of the SAEs were reported as single events. The majority of the events were considered moderate (6 out of 18) or severe (11 out of 18), but none of the SAEs were judged by the investigator to be possibly or probably related to trial product. All except one (a SAE of PT Osteoarthritis) were reported as resolved.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

Study 4381 SAEs were reported by 12.1% of the subjects in the semaglutide group compared to 9.1% in the placebo group. The SAEs were distributed across multiple SOCs. All were assessed by the investigators as being unlikely related to treatment and all except one subject were reported as recovered or recovering. The event that was not recovered was a malignant neoplasm (diffuse large bcell lymphoma) in the semaglutide s.c. 0.4 mg arm, which was deemed unlikely related to trial product.

## 5.4.5. Discontinuation due to adverse events

## Study 4553

Premature discontinuation . In trial 4553 at interim, AEs leading to premature discontinuation of trial product were reported by a low proportion of subjects in both treatment groups (semaglutide 2.4 mg: 2.6%, 2.4 events per 100 PYE; placebo: 3.3%, 2.6 events per 100 PYE). The majority of the events were non-serious, and mild or moderate in severity.

AEs leading to premature discontinuation of trial product were distributed across multiple SOCs. Premature discontinuation of trial product due to gastrointestinal AEs was reported by a low number of subjects, with slightly higher proportion of subjects with semaglutide 2.4 mg (1.6%) compared to placebo (0.5%). The majority of the events in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group occurred during the doseescalation period.

SAEs leading to premature discontinuation of trial product were reported for 0.8% of subjects with semaglutide 2.4 mg and 1.5% of subjects with placebo, with event types spread across multiple SOCs and PTs.

Temporary interruption . AEs leading to temporary interruption of trial product were reported by a higher proportion of subjects and at higher event rates with semaglutide 2.4 mg (12.0%, 13.2 events per 100 PYE) compared to placebo (6.8%, 7.5 events per 100 PYE). The majority of the events were non-serious, and mild or moderate in severity. The imbalance was mainly driven by gastrointestinal AEs, which accounted for around half of the events leading to temporary interruption of trial product in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group. The remaining events were distributed across multiple SOCs.

SAEs leading to temporary interruption of trial product were reported by comparable proportions of subjects treated with semaglutide 2.4 mg (3.3%) compared to placebo (2.3%).

Dose reduction . AEs leading to dose reduction were reported by a higher proportion of subjects and at higher event rates for semaglutide 2.4 mg (10.0%, 11.8 events per 100 PYE) compared to placebo (2.0%, 1.8 events per 100 PYE). These events were spread across multiple SOCs and PTs, with the most common PTs being nausea, vomiting, diarrhoea, decreased appetite and weight decreased.

## Supportive studies

Study 4296 The proportion of subjects with AEs leading to premature discontinuation of trial product was higher in the semaglutide 0.2 mg once daily group (12.8%) than in the other treatment groups (semaglutide 0.1 mg: 3.8%; semaglutide 0.4 mg: 4.9%; placebo: 5.0%). The majority of events were non-serious, and mild or moderate in severity. Gastrointestinal AEs accounted for approximately half of the AEs leading to premature discontinuation of trial product in the semaglutide groups. The remaining AEs were single events reported by single subjects, distributed across multiple SOCs with no apparent pattern.

AEs leading to temporary interruption of trial product were reported in ~ 15% of subjects on semaglutide vs 6.3% of subjects on placebo, with Aes mainly belonging to the SOCs Gastrointestinal disorders, Infections and infestations, and Investigations. AEs leading to dose reduction were reported

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

for 1 subject with semaglutide 0.2 mg (1 AE) and 3 subjects with semaglutide 0.4 mg once daily (4 AEs).

Study 4492 AEs leading to premature discontinuation of trial product were reported by 3 subjects (6.4%) with semaglutide 2.4 mg and no subjects with placebo, mainly due to the PT nausea. Temporary interruption of trial product was reported by 2 subjects, 1(2.1%) subject in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group (4 AEs) and 1(2.1%) subject in the placebo group (1 AE), and dose reduction was reported in 4 subjects in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group (6 AEs) and 1 subject in the placebo group (2 AEs). The most common SOC was Gastrointestinal disorders.

Study 4381 One (1) AE leading to premature discontinuation of trial product was reported by 1 (3.0%) subject with semaglutide 0.4 mg once daily, no events were reported with placebo. The AE reported in the semaglutide 0.4 mg group was diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, reported on day 92 and assessed as unlikely related to trial product. A total of 4 subjects (3 (9.0%) on semaglutide 0.4 mg OD vs 1 (3.0%) on placebo) reported AEs leading to temporary interruption of trial product, and a dose reduction was reported for 2 subjects with semaglutide. All events in the semaglutide group were reported within the SOC Gastrointestinal disorders.

## 5.4.6. Safety in special populations

## AEs across age-groups

In study 4553, a total of 210 (26.3%) out of 800 subjects randomised to semaglutide 2.4 mg were aged &gt;= 65 years. AEs were reported at similar frequency across age-groups &lt;45, 45-64, and &gt;=65 years of age, in ~85% of subjects, leading to dose reduction in a higher proportion of patients &gt;=65 years (13.8% vs ~8.5%) and discontinuation of semaglutide in 3.3% vs 0.8% at age &lt;45 years. A higher proportion of subjects &gt;= 65 years of age on semaglutide reported SAEs, in 14.8% at age &gt;=65 years vs 10.8% &lt; 45 years, as well as severe AEs (11.9% vs 5.0%).

Across age, the PT decreased appetite was reported by a higher proportion of subjects in the subgroups with high age ( ≥ 65 years) in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group compared to the lower ages. The proportion of subjects reporting decreased appetite increased with increased age. This was not reflected in the matched placebo groups which had comparable proportions across the age groups.

Additionally, AEs by age subgroups in older patients (&lt;65; ≥ 65-&lt;75; and ≥ 75-&lt;85 ) have shown no remarkable differences by SOCs or PTs. While more AEs occurred in older patients, the increase occurred similarly in both groups.

## Intrinsic factors

The potential impact of various intrinsic factors on the safety profile of semaglutide 2.4 mg versus placebo was evaluated based on data from the on-treatment period for trial 4553 (SAS [interim]), by comparing the AE-reporting pattern across subgroups within the semaglutide 2.4 mg group, as well as any subgroup differences in the SOC/PT reporting. The results across subgroups should be interpreted with caution; the total trial population was randomised per treatment group and stratified by T2D status and fibrosis stage at baseline only. The trial population was not stratified by the remaining intrinsic factors, hence uneven distributions within subgroups are observed. Furthermore, the number of subjects in some of the subgroups are low, increasing the likelihood of chance findings.

## AEs by fibrosis stage at baseline

MASH F2 vs NASH F3. In study 4553, AEs (in 90.2% of subjects with MASH F2 vs 85.1% of subjects with MASH F3), SAEs (20.1% vs 11.0%) and severe AEs (13.2% vs 7.7%) were reported at a slightly increased frequency in subjects with F2 as compared to F3. The same tendency was seen in the matching placebo subgroups for overall AEs, whereas comparable proportion of subjects with placebo reported SAEs and severe AEs across the F2 and F3 subgroups.

Slight variations between the F2 and F3 subgroups were seen for reporting of common PTs in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group. The overall higher reporting in the F2 subgroup was mainly driven by events within the SOCs Gastrointestinal disorders, and Infections and infestations, driven by the PTs

MASH F1-F3 vs MASH F2-F3. Across treatment groups, the pattern of AEs and SAEs for subjects with F2/F3 was similar to that in the overall population.

The higher event rates in the semaglutide group were primarily driven by gastrointestinal AEs. Three subjects in the semaglutide group vs none in the placebo group discontinued treatment due to AEs.

All the reported SAEs were assessed as unlikely related to trial product by the investigators. No decompensation events took place in the trial and stable disease scores were reported (e.g., MELD score and Child-Pugh score).

Five (5) subjects (14.3%) in the semaglutide group reported severe or blood glucose (BG) confirmed symptomatic episodes (Novo Nordisk classification) versus 3 subjects (16.7%) in the placebo group.

## AEs by BMI at baseline

30 &lt;= BMI &lt; 35

25 &lt;= BMI &lt; 30

(N=116)

(N-173)

Baseline BMI sub-group

In study 4553, a total of 113, 115, 249 and 323 subjects with BMI &lt;27, 27-&lt;30, 30-&lt;35, and &gt;=35 kg/m2 were treated with semaglutide. AEs, SAEs and severe AEs were reported at comparable frequency across BMI ranges, in 84.1% to 88.7%, 12.0%t to 14.2%, and 7.0% to 9.6% of subjects on semaglutide, respectively. Interruption or reduction of semaglutide dose was reported more frequently in subjects with BMI &lt; 27 kg/m 2 , in 21.2% vs 8.0%-13.7% and 21.2% vs 5.9%-12.2%, respectively. This pattern was not fully reflected in the matching placebo subgroups, where overall AEs were reported by a higher proportion of subjects in the low-BMI subgroup; however, based on a low number of subjects.

In the semaglutide 2.4 mg group, the SOC Gastrointestinal disorders (and the most common PTs: nausea, diarrhoea, and constipation) were all reported by a lower proportion of subjects in the low BMI subgroup compared to the high-BMI subgroup, with varying reporting patterns in the matching placebo groups. The same was seen for the SOC Infections and infestations (driven by the PT COVID-19), which was reported by a lower proportion of subjects in the low-BMI subgroup with semaglutide 2.4 mg compared to the high BMI subgroup. In contrast, the PT decreased appetite was reported by a higher proportion of subjects in the low-BMI subgroup (17.7%) compared to the high-BMI subgroup (13.4%). This difference was not reflected in the matching placebo subgroups.

An increase in some AEs, dose reductions, and interruptions with lower BMI may be driven by higher drug exposure and greater susceptibility to gastrointestinal related AEs.

## AEs across T2D at baseline

In study 4553, a total of 446 (55.8%) vs 222 (56.2%) subjects with T2D at baseline were randomised to semaglutide or placebo. When analysed across baseline T2D, AEs, SAEs and AEs leading to early

35 &lt; BMI

BMI &lt; 25

(N=36)

35 &lt;= BMI

(N=209)

(N=209)

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

discontinuation of semaglutide 2.4 mg was reported in 87.0% vs 85.3%, 13.0% vs 13.0%, and 2.5% vs 2.8% of subjects with vs without baseline T2D, and for subjects on placebo in 76.6% vs 81.5%, 14.4% vs 11.0%, and 1.7% vs 2.0% of subjects with vs without baseline T2D.

## AEs across sex

Female subjects treated with semaglutide 2.4 mg reported AEs at comparable frequency as male subjects, by 400 (88.7%) vs 290 (83.1%) subjects. However, adverse event lead to dose-reduction in a slightly higher proportion of women, 11.1% vs 8.6% of subjects and discontinuation of semaglutide in 3.3% vs 1.7% of subjects. SAEs were reported by 15.5% vs 9.7% of subjects.

Main differences were seen for GI AEs (72.9% vs 65.0%), and Infections and Infestations (50.6% vs 37.0%). GI events reported more frequently in females were nausea (41.2% vs 29.5%) and vomiting (20.8% vs 15.5%). However, no imbalance was seen for GI events of diarrhoea, constipation, or dyspepsia. Covid-19 (19.7% vs 12.3%) and UTI (8.4% vs 1.1%) were reported more frequently by female subjects.

## AEs across race

When comparing across race in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group, a higher proportion of subjects of Asian race reported SAEs compared to Whites, in 17.2% vs 11.3% subjects, respectively. That was not reflected in the matched placebo subgroups which had comparable proportions for the two races. The remaining subgroups for race were too small for comparisons.

## Extrinsic factors

The potential impact of extrinsic factors (region and trial-product pen-injector) on the safety profile of semaglutide 2.4 mg was evaluated by comparing the AE-reporting pattern across subgroups within the semaglutide 2.4 mg group, as well as any subgroup differences in the SOC/PT reporting. Overall, AEs by region showed comparable AE-reporting patterns across subgroups. No noteworthy differences between subgroups were seen when assessing AEs on SOC and PT levels.

AEs by trial-product pen-injector generally showed a higher proportion of subjects reporting AEs in the DV3396 subgroup compared to the PDS290 subgroup; however, this difference was as expected because subjects using PDS290 were enrolled later in the trial, hence there is a higher total exposure for DV3396 (1641 PYE) compared to PDS290 (321.6 PYE). For general AE reporting including SAEs and severe AEs, similar reporting patterns was seen for both semaglutide 2.4 mg and the placebo group.

## 5.4.7. Immunological events

See section on Adverse events of special Interest, Immunogenicity .

## 5.4.8. Safety related to drug-drug interactions and other interactions

Interactions with other medicinal products have been assessed for the use of semaglutide in the T2D and weight management programme (Ozempic, Wegovy). In vitro studies have shown very low potential for semaglutide to inhibit or induce CYP enzymes, or to inhibit drug transporters.

As with other GLP-1 receptor agonists, semaglutide may delay gastric emptying and could potentially influence the absorption of concomitantly administered oral medicinal products. No clinically relevant effect on the rate of gastric emptying has been observed with semaglutide 2.4 mg. The potential effect of semaglutide on the absorption of co-administered oral medications was studied in trials at semaglutide 1.0 mg steady-state exposure (NN9535) as part of the T2D clinical development programme. No clinically relevant drug-drug interactions with semaglutide were observed based on the

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

evaluated medications. Therefore, no dose adjustment is required when co-administered with semaglutide.

## 5.4.9. Vital signs and laboratory findings

In this summary, the evaluations of laboratory data from the central laboratory are based on the ontreatment period for trials 4296 and 4553 (interim evaluation). Further, abnormal laboratory values deemed clinically relevant by the investigator (i.e., reported as AEs) are included in the evaluation.

## Summary of clinical laboratory evaluations

Haematology . In trial 4553 (interim evaluation) and 4296, haematology parameters assessed included haemoglobin, leucocytes, erythrocytes, thrombocytes and differential cell counts (basophils, eosinophils, lymphocytes, monocytes and neutrophils). No clinically relevant mean changes in haematology parameters were observed from baseline to week 72 in any of the treatment groups.

Biochemistry . In study 4553 for the majority of the laboratory parameters assessed, the mean values remained stable from baseline to week 72 with both semaglutide and placebo. Mean levels of ALT and AST decreased more with semaglutide than with placebo, and amylase and lipase levels increased. Ferritin decreased in both semaglutide and placebo groups.

Across parameters, no relevant outlier patterns were observed, and no safety concerns were identified based on the central laboratory assessments nor on data obtained from local laboratory assessments.

Liver-related parameters (AST, ALT, ALP, INR, MELD, bilirubin and albumin), parameters related to renal failure (eGFR and creatinine), and pancreatic enzymes (lipase and amylase) are described in subsections Hepatic disorders including potential DILI events , Acute renal failure and Acute pancreatitis in section Adverse Events of Special Interest.

Ferritin. In trial 4553, the geometric mean values of ferritin decreased from baseline to week 72 for both semaglutide 2.4 mg and placebo. At baseline, geometric mean value of ferritin was 142,92 in the semaglutide group and 135,38 in the placebo group. At week 72, the ratio to baseline was 0,67 in the semaglutide group and 0,79 in the placebo group.

In trial 4296, dose-dependent ferritin decrease can be seen in ratio to baseline: sema 0.1mg: 0.660, sema 0.2 mg: 0.617, sema 0.4 mg: 0.603 vs placebo: 0.713.

## Vital signs, physical findings, and other observations related to safety

In trial 4553, assessment of vital signs included pulse rate and blood pressure (SBP and DBP), which are evaluated as part of the safety focus area for cardiovascular disorders. Change in SBP and DBP from baseline to week 72 were also included as part of the efficacy assessment. Eye examinations are evaluated in subsection Retinal disorders.

In trial 4296, assessment of vital signs included pulse rate, blood pressure (SBP and DBP) and ECGs, which are evaluated as part of the safety focus area for cardiovascular disorders.

## 5.4.10. Post-marketing experience

Semaglutide s.c. 2.4 mg once weekly for weight management is marketed as Wegovy, with the first marketing authorisation granted on 04 June 2021.

Semaglutide s.c. for T2D has been marketed as Ozempic, with the first marketing authorisation received on 05 December 2017. Oral semaglutide for T2D has been marketed as Rybelsus, with the first marketing authorisation received on 20 September 2019.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

The post marketing safety data for semaglutide received by the applicant are made available in the Periodic Safety Update Report/Periodic Benefit Risk Evaluation Report (PSUR/PBRER) according to the regulatory requirements. The latest submitted PSUR/PBRER for semaglutide provides a critical analysis of the safety and benefit-risk balance for Wegovy, Ozempic and Rybelsus (reporting interval: 01 Jun 2023 to 31 May 2024). Based on cumulative data from more than 40,000 subjects exposed to semaglutide in completed clinical trials and post-marketing data from ~20 million patients treated the applicant evaluated that no additional pharmacovigilance and risk minimisation activities were required.

## 5.4.11. In vitro biomarker test for patient selection for safety

Not Applicable

## 5.4.12. Overall discussion and conclusions on clinical safety

## 5.4.12.1. Discussion

## 5.4.12.1.1. Overall assessment of available safety data

Main safety data on semaglutide for the treatment of MASH are derived from pivotal study 4553 (ESSENCE) in patients with MASH F2-F3, with semaglutide dosed at 2.4 mg s.c. once weekly, similar to the proposed dose in the SmPC. Supportive data are obtained from study 4296, in patients with MASH F1-F3 with semaglutide dosed at either 0.1 mg, 0.2 mg or 0.4 mg OD, study 4492 in patients with biopsy-confirmed MASH and compensated cirrhosis (F4c) with semaglutide dosed at 2.4 mg once weekly, and study 4381 in patients with NAFLD and increased liver stiffness with semaglutide dosed at 0.4 mg OD. In line with EMA reflection paper on regulatory requirements for the development of medicinal products for non-alcoholic steatohepatitis (NASH) , the investigated population in the clinical program therefore covers non-cirrhotic MASH (fibrosis stages 2-3), and compensated cirrhotic MASH (fibrosis stage 4), which reflects grades of MASH that are associated with risk of progression to ESLD, liver transplantation and death and may also be related to differential risks of adverse events. Since the study population as well as the semaglutide dosing regimen differed between studies, data were presented separated per study and no pooled data were included. Results from study 4553 concern a predefined interim analysis, as per data cut-off date of 05 September 2024.

All studies were randomised double-blind placebo-controlled studies, enabling estimation of the frequency of adverse events. The lack of an active control is acceptable since there wasn't any approved medicinal treatment for MASH at the beginning of the clinical trials. Despite blinding, patients and investigators may still have been aware of the allocated treatment, due to the weight loss associated with semaglutide, however, potential bias introduced was mitigated by additional blinded adjudication of selected events in study 4553. Two separate analysis data sets were defined, based on the observation period. The definition of the in-trial observation period, reflecting time followed in the trial, and the on-treatment observation period, reflecting time on trial medication, is acceptable. The follow-up period after discontinuation of trial treatment, of 7 days after last dose for laboratory assessments and physical examination, and of 7 weeks for adverse events and hypoglycaemic episodes, is based on expected levels of systemic exposure of semaglutide for approximately 7 weeks after the last dose based on the elimination half-life of 1 week and is acceptable.

In addition, extensive safety data are available from prior clinical development programmes of

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

semaglutide in T2D and in weight management, as well as post-marketing data covering ~20 million patients. However, when translating these results to the proposed indication in patients with MASH, differences in dosing regimens (with maximum semaglutide dose of 2.0 mg once weekly in T2D, and oral administration of Rybelsus of 4 to 50 mg once daily) and in study populations must be taken into consideration, and most relevant safety data on semaglutide at a dose of 2.4 mg once weekly come from semaglutide for weight management (Wegovy). Though PK-modelling data demonstrated a comparable PK exposure on 0.4 mg once daily and 2.4 mg once weekly, still the safety profile may differ, as daily administration of semaglutide has been associated with increased frequency of (gastro-intestinal) AEs.

Exposure . At data-cut-off date of 05 September 2024, a total of 1585 subjects were exposed to the trial product in phase 3a trials 4553 [interim]), and phase 2 trials 4296 and 4492, 1086 subjects to semaglutide and 499 subjects to placebo. Total exposure to semaglutide in the on-treatment observation period was 1716.6 PYE. In addition, in study 4381, 66 subjects were exposed to the trial product, with 33 subjects exposed to semaglutide.

In the pivotal study 4553 in the target population with MASH F2-F3 and at the proposed dosing regimen of 2.4 mg once weekly, a total of 800 subjects was treated with semaglutide, with 643 subjects treated for at least 6 months, and a total of 542 subjects treated for at least 12 months, thereby fulfilling requirements of ICH-E1. The mean (SD) exposure time for subjects in SAS (interim) was 85.7 (50.4) weeks for the on-treatment observation period, 87.1 (50.7) weeks on semaglutide, and 82.8 (49.9) weeks on placebo. This may cause a slight overestimation of frequency of adverse events on semaglutide. However this effect is attenuated by the timing of most adverse events, that are reported mainly during the initial treatment phase.

Adverse events . In the pivotal study 4553, AEs were reported at higher frequency in subjects on semaglutide as compared with placebo (86.3% vs 78.7%), predominantly gastrointestinal AEs (69.5% versus 46.8%), mainly nausea (36.1% vs 12.4%), diarrhoea (26.8% vs 12.2%), constipation (22.1% vs 7.8%) and vomiting (18.5% vs 5.6%). Most AEs reported were graded as mild or moderate (in 80.3% vs 72.7%, and 47.8% vs 42.8%) and only a limited proportion of AEs was graded severe, with no imbalance for semaglutide vs placebo, 8.9% vs 8.4%.

Adverse drug reactions i.e. adverse events deemed probably or possibly related were reported in 68.6% of subjects on semaglutide vs 40.8% subjects on placebo. ADRs were graded as mild, moderate or severe, in 62.5% vs 35.9%, 26.9% vs 10.9% and 2.8% vs 1.0%, respectively. Most ADRs occurring at increased frequency on semaglutide concerned gastrointestinal disorders, in 60.3% vs 30.9% of subjects, including nausea (34.1% vs 10.9%), diarrhoea (20.3% vs 10.4%), constipation (17.3% vs 5.3%), vomiting (15.9% vs 3.3%), dyspepsia (6.9% vs 2.3%), abdominal distention (6.5% vs 2.5%), and gastro-oesophageal reflux disease (5.9% vs 0.5%). Also, decreased appetite (13.6% vs 2.3%), fatigue (6.4% vs 2.8%), headache (4.9% vs 3.0 %), and weight decreased (4.1% vs 0.5%) were reported at increased frequency on semaglutide. The frequency of nausea-events increased dose-dependently on semaglutide, whereas no clear dose relationship was observed for diarrhoea, vomiting or constipation or other adverse events. ADRs graded as severe and reported in &gt; 2 subjects mainly concerned gastro-intestinal AEs (nausea, diarrhoea, and vomiting). Semaglutide and associated weight loss have been associated with reduction of bone density. However, the mechanisms for this risk are not fully elucidated yet. Given the limited number of back pain events and that all three subjects had pre-existing risk factors for back pain (i.e., history of spinal stenosis and degenerative disc disease, history of osteopenia, and myalgia) the current data does not allow to establish a decisive connection between semaglutide and back pain nor reduced bone density.

In study 4553, according to protocol semaglutide was started at a dose of 0.25 mg once weekly, to be escalated based on tolerability, at intervals of 4 weeks to 0.5 mg, 1.0 mg, 1.7 mg, and reaching

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

the final dose of 2.4 mg once weekly at week 16. The target dose of 2.4 mg was reached by 508 (81.7%) subjects treated with semaglutide at week 24, and remained stable thereafter, with 479 (84.0%) at target dose at week 72. The majority of adverse events was reported within the doseescalation period, though events of nausea were reported throughout the trial duration. Temporary discontinuation or dose reduction of semaglutide were reported at increased frequency compared with placebo, in 12% vs 6.8% and 10.0% vs 2.0%, respectively. However premature discontinuation of study treatment was only required in a small proportion of subjects without imbalance between study-arms (3.3% vs 2.6 %).

A total of 9 deaths were reported in study 4553 (interim), 3 subjects in the semaglutide group and 6 subjects on placebo. All fatal events were assessed as unlikely related to trial product, which is agreed with.

Serious AEs were reported at comparable frequency in semaglutide (13.0%) vs placebo (12.9%), with comparable grading of severity for both treatment arms, and deemed possibly or potentially related in 2.4% vs 1.3% of subjects. A dose reduction was required in a limited number of subjects ( n=1) and study drug withdrawal in 0.8% vs 1.5% of subjects. Fatal SAE was reported in 0.4% vs 1.3% of subjects. The SAEs mainly concerned infections and gastro-intestinal disorders. Serious infections/inflammation seen in more than one case on semaglutide were appendicitis (n= 3), diverticulitis (n= 3), and Covid-19 (n= 6). Gastro-intestinal disorders seen more than once were intestinal polyps (n= 5), ischaemic colitis (n= 2), gastritis (n= 2), nausea (n= 2), and pancreatitis (n= 3). The number of events is low, without clear imbalance between treatment arms. Of note, in a recent pharmacovigilance study based on the FDA adverse event reporting system (FAERS) 10 cases of serious (0.56%) and 2 cases of nonserious appendicitis (0.05%) associated with semaglutide over the span of 2018 to 2022 were reported ( Shu et al, Frontiers in Public Health, 2022 ). However, the signal of appendicitis has already been discussed in semaglutide PSUSAs (PSUSA/00010671/202405), resulting in no updates to the label of the product. The imbalance in the number of appendicitis events is in line with previous studies, and it is not considered sufficient to raise a safety issue at this stage.

Supportive studies . In supportive study 4296 in MASH F1-F3, a comparable frequency of adverse events was reported on semaglutide 0.1 mg, 0.2 mg and 0.4 mg OD. SAEs were reported at increased rate on semaglutide 0.2 mg OD, in 19.2% of subjects, vs 3.8% and 4.9% subjects at 0.1 mg and 0.4 mg OD. However, SAEs were distributed over multiple SOCs, and increased frequency at semaglutide 0.2 mg was mainly seen for neoplasms (benign or malignant) and nervous system disorders without clustering of events, which is likely due to chance finding. Safety data of study 4381 in NAFLD with increased liver stiffness with semaglutide dosed at 0.4 mg OD, in general were consistent with findings in the pivotal study 4553. In supportive study 4492 in MASH F4c with semaglutide dosed 2.4 mg once weekly AEs and SAEs in the semaglutide group were reported at a comparable frequency as in study 4553 in MASH F2/F3, in 89.4% vs 86.3% and 12.8% vs 13.0%, respectively. Severe AEs were reported at higher frequency in subjects with MASH F4 in study 4492, by 17% of subjects as compared with 8.9% of MASH F2-F3 subjects in study 4553. This mainly concerned gastro-intestinal AEs, however without clustering of specific events and a causal relation with semaglutide is deemed unlikely.

No new AEs/safety concerns were identified for the MASH population as compared to the other semaglutide indications, despite the small differences in AE frequencies.

Hepatotoxicity and drug-induced liver injury (DILI) are important aspects for drugs intended for treatment of MASH. Based on prior results in T2D and weight management, semaglutide was not known to be hepatotoxic. In the pivotal study 4553, subjects with ALT / AST &gt; 5xULN or ALP&gt; 2xULN were excluded. Though signs of hepatic dysfunction were seen in most subjects, as reflected by

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

elevated baseline AST or ALT up to 3x ULN in 68.2% and 49.8% of subjects, only a limited number of patients with more severe elevated AST, ALT or ALP &gt; 3x ULN were enrolled (90, 42, and 45 subjects, respectively). During the treatment phase, mean values for ALT and AST decreased from baseline to week 72 in both treatment groups, whereas mean ALP decreased only in the semaglutide group, as also reported in the clinical efficacy section. Total bilirubin, albumin, and INR remained stable over time in both groups. Also, the MELD score remained unchanged up to 72 weeks in both groups.

Numerically, the frequency of hepatic events was lower on semaglutide as compared with placebo (5.0% vs 7.8%). Most hepatic events were mild or moderately severe, and serious hepatic event was reported in 1 (0.1%) vs 3 (0.8%) subjects. No patient qualified for either Hy's law or potential DILI. Based on these results, no signal of hepatic toxicity is seen in study 4553.

Results from supportive studies 4296 in MASH F1-F3 and study 4381 in subjects with NAFLD with increased liver stiffness are consistent.

Gall-bladder-related disorders such as cholelithiasis are associated with NAFLD, obesity, and rapid and large weight loss. In subjects treated with GLP-1 RA this risk was increased in a dose-dependent way and acute cholelithiasis is considered a class risk for GLP-1 RA. In study 4553, gallbladder related disorders were reported at slightly increased frequency on semaglutide (2.5% vs 1.5% of subjects), most frequently concerning cholelithiasis (1.4% vs 0.8%). Also, symptomatic acute gallbladder disease was seen at increased frequency on semaglutide, in 5 vs 2 subjects, reported as serious in 1 subject on semaglutide and placebo, each. The frequency of cholelithiasis on semaglutide was comparable with the frequency on semaglutide for weight management, in 1.6% of subjects, and no increase in severity nor in the required invasive treatments was seen in the MASH studies. However, in patients with hepatic cirrhosis symptomatic cholelithiasis and cholecystectomy have been associated with increased risks ( Malick et al, 2021; Perkins et al. 2004 ), and therefore events related to cholelithiasis may have a more unfavourable course in MASH patients. Also, the risk of developing cholelithiasis may increase during prolonged semaglutide treatment. Though the cholelithiasis-related events reported in the MASH studies so far do not raise additional concern as compared with semaglutide for other indications, the number of investigated subjects and the duration of follow-up are too limited to allow for robust conclusions. Data on cholelithiasis and treatments from the T2D and weight management programs as well as from post-marketing experience was provided, showing that the rates of cholelithiasis identified in study 4553 are in line with the known profile of semaglutide and the risk is adequately reflected in the SmPC. Additional data on prolonged semaglutide treatment will become available following completion of study 4553, which will relevantly contribute to further estimation of the risks of cholelithiasis in MASH subjects.

Acute pancreatitis is considered a class risk associated with the use of GLP-1 RA, and MASH has been associated with increased severity of acute pancreatitis ( Yoon et al,. 2017; Váncsa et al. 2020 ). In study 4553, amylase and lipase levels slightly rose during semaglutide treatment. A total of 3 events were categorised as SAE, 2 (0.3%) vs 1 (0.3%). Though numbers are small and so far do not signal a considerable safety risk, the high proportion of adverse events of acute pancreatitis graded as serious do reflect the potential severity of this condition, and also, the wide range of ratio to baseline values for lipase as well as amylase up to 44 in study 4553, and up to 88 in study 4296 does signal relevant effects on pancreatic functioning. Similar to gall-bladder related adverse events, acute pancreatitis may also be expected to occur throughout long term treatment as opposed to other GI AEs that were mainly seen during the initial treatment phase. The number of subjects investigated in the MASH studies and duration of follow-up are still limited to fully explore this safety risk in MASH patients. At this point acute pancreatitis is not seen at increased frequency on semaglutide as compared with placebo in MASH subjects, and the reported event rates are in line with the rates of other semaglutide studies for weight management and T2D. Nevertheless, additional data during prolonged semaglutide treatment that will come available following completion of study 4553 will

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

relevantly contribute to further estimation of this potential safety issue.

Cardiovascular events . Though increased pulse rate is known as a class effect of GLP-1 Ras, the mean increase of pulse rate on semaglutide of 2 beats/min, and similar frequency of events related to palpitations on semaglutide as compared with placebo is reassuring. No safety signals were seen due to cardiovascular events, and the reduction of SBP of 5.39 mmHg on semaglutide may be of additional clinical benefit.

Neoplasms . In a review by EMA to evaluate the risk of pancreatic adverse events associated with use of GLP1-based therapies no evidence for an increased risk of pancreatic malignancy was found. However, uncertainties remained to exist since long-term consequences of stimulation of beta-cells and suppression of alpha cells as well as possible effects on exocrine pancreas were considered largely unknown. Therefore, pancreatic cancer was classified as potential class risk. Currently, based on data in T2D, weight management, and post-marketing no evidence of increased risk of pancreatic cancer has been seen. In study 4553, malignant neoplasms were reported in 14 (1.8%) subjects on semaglutide vs 10 (2.5%) subjects on placebo, without clustering of specific types of malignancies or evident imbalance between treatment groups. One case of ductal adenocarcinoma of the pancreas in study 4553 was reported as considered unlikely related to study drug. Though no signal of increased risk of malignancy on semaglutide is seen, pancreatic cancer remains in the RMP as important potential risk, in line with the EC regulation 726/2004 Article 5(3) referral procedure from 2013 (EMEA/H/A-5(3)/1369) .

Renal cysts were reported at increased frequency on semaglutide, in 13 (1.6%) vs 2 (0.5%) of subjects. Information is to some extent limited since additional information for renal cysts was not systematically collected. From the information available it has been established that there were 11 cases of single cysts and 5 cases of multiple cysts. Simple cysts are classified as level I-II in the Bosniak classification, which have a negligible risk of malignancy. One of the events was classified as a SAE, and all 16 were mild and considered unlikely related to trial product. None of the events did lead to treatment discontinuation or changes in dose, except for 1 subject. The majority of subjects reporting renal cysts co-reported gastrointestinal AEs or other AEs (e.g. musculoskeletal disorders or nephrolithiasis), which are AEs that could lead to imaging and thereby incidental findings of renal cysts. This hypothesis cannot be confirmed with the data available, although it is considered plausible. No safety signal on renal cysts has ever been raised for semaglutide in other populations/indications, which supports an unlikely relation between semaglutide and renal cysts.

In trial 4296 neoplasms (benign or malignant) formed in 12.5%, 14.1% and 17.5 % of subjects on semaglutide 0.1 mg, 0.2 mg and 0.4 mg OD, respectively. In the placebo group neoplasms formed in 7.5 % of subjects. Though numbers of subjects are small, overall neoplasm formations seems to be dose-dependent, including gastrointestinal neoplasms in 1.3%, 5.1% and 6.2% of subjects on semaglutide vs 0 subjects on placebo, and hepatobiliary neoplasms (in 0, 1.3%, and 2.5% of subjects on semaglutide vs 0 on placebo). Renal cysts don't show dose-dependency (in 3.8%, 1.3% and 0% of subjects on semaglutide vs 1.3% of subjects on placebo). The Applicant was asked to discuss the differences in neoplasm formation between the 2 trials) and also discuss the possible dosagedependence in trial 4296.

In trial 4296, neoplasm formation AEs were reported by a higher proportion of subjects in the semaglutide groups than in the placebo group. However, this evaluation was based on relatively small treatment groups and numerically few events. The applicant considered that the number of neoplasms reported are too low to conclude on dose dependence and this was accepted.

According to the interim safety evaluation of trial 4553 based on 1195 subjects, AEs of neoplasms were reported by comparable proportions of subjects and event rates in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group (8.5%, 6.3 events per 100 PYO) and in the placebo group (9.9%, 8.4 events per 100 PYO).

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

This is in line with the development programme of Wegovy and data from the large, long-term CVOT EX9536-4388.

Renal function and AKI . Adverse events of acute renal failure were reported infrequently, at comparable rate on semaglutide and placebo, in 0.6% vs 0.5% respectively, which is reassuring. However,  a numerically slightly higher number of serious cases of AKI were reported on semaglutide versus none on placebo.  These cases concerned AKI stage 3, likely due to prerenal causes since cases improved following measures to correct BP and hydration. These were most probably caused by the known GI AEs leading to dehydration. This risk was already reflected in the SmPC.

Adverse events of urolithiasis seem to have been reported frequently, though it is difficult to get a complete overview due to a variety of PT's that can be used, including nephrolithiasis, urolithiasis, and hydronephrosis, and also cases of AKI/CKD may be related. The events of urolithiasis occurred slightly more frequently in study 4553 (semaglutide vs placebo: 1.8 vs 1.3 events per 100 PYE) compared to other clinical studies performed with semaglutide (in weight management and T2D programs, semaglutide vs placebo, range: 1.5 - 0.17 vs 1.4 - 0.09 events per 100 PYE). The increase, however, is considered small, and it may be explained by the different populations. In study 4553, MASH population with fibrosis stages 2 and 3 were included, and non-alcoholic liver disease has been associated with an increased risk of urolithiasis ( Miao, Int. J. Med. Sci. 2025 ). A slight difference is noted in study 4553 between semaglutide and placebo, although the difference is also small, and it is not possible to conclude an effect of semaglutide. Regarding hydronephrosis and acute renal failure, the event rates in study 4553 are also in line with other semaglutide studies.

Hypoglycaemia . In study 4553, ~55% of patients enrolled had a concomitant diagnosis of T2D in both treatment arms, and mean baseline HbA1c of 7.2% (55 mmol/mol) vs 6.9% (52 mmol/mol) was close to clinical practice target level of 54-58 mmol/mol for subjects with T2D for &lt;10 years treated with antidiabetic medication (ADA criteria). Diabetes treatment consisted of metformin in most cases (41.4% vs 39.7%) and included insulin in 6.4% vs 8.7% of subjects and sulfonylureas in 9.5% vs 8.4% of subjects. In study 4553, a substantial proportion of subjects was concomitantly treated with DPP-4 inhibitors (baseline use in 9.9% vs 8.4% of subjects) and semaglutide, despite it is not advised to use GLP1-RA and DPP4-inhibitors concomitantly ( Davies et al. Management of Hyperglycemia in Type 2 Diabetes, 2018.A Consensus Report by the American Diabetes Association (ADA) and the European Association for the Study of Diabetes (EASD )).

The proportion of subjects with T2D at baseline reporting hypoglycaemic events was higher in the semaglutide group, 19.3% vs 13.1% on placebo. Also, serious, clinically important hypoglycaemia (level 2, &lt;3.0 mmol/l) and severe hypoglycaemia (requiring assistance from another person) were reported more frequently on semaglutide, in 6.1% vs 5.0% and 2.2% vs 0.5% of subjects, respectively. Given the definitions of level 2 or 3 hypoglycaemia, a number of events could be considered as level 2 instead of level 3. As such, the events are assessed as pooled data. The events reported with the use of semaglutide are in line with other semaglutide trials, although higher than in other studies. For instance, similar rates were reported in STEP 2 trial, where hypoglycaemic events level 2/level 3 occurred in 6.5% (10.9 events per 100 PYE) with semaglutide and 2.5% (3.2 events per 100 PYE) with placebo. In the study protocol of study 4553, no instructions seem to be included on management of antidiabetic medication at initiation of semaglutide, though in clinical practice reduction or discontinuation of insulin and /or sulfonylurea is advised, based on HbA1c levels. In study 4553, in MASH subjects without T2D hypoglycaemia was only rarely reported without imbalance between treatment groups (in 0.3% vs 0.6% of subjects). However, AEs of hypoglycaemia were evaluated based on a predefined MedDRA search only, as opposed to recording using a specific hypoglycaemic-episode form in patients with T2D, and therefore may be at risk of underreporting. In order to further estimate the risk of hypoglycaemia in patients without T2D, the applicant was asked to provide all the adverse events of the non-diabetic subjects in 4553 trial which could be related to

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

hypoglycaemia in the semaglutide and in the placebo group. Slightly higher frequencies and event rates were observed for the non-T2DM (NT2DM) population in the semaglutide 2.4 mg arm compared to the NT2DM population in the placebo arm with regards to frequencies, event rates, frequency of probably/possibly related events and discontinuation. Upon request, the Applicant clarified that there were 1 and 2 patients in the semaglutide and placebo groups respectively, who were NT2DM at baseline, but were diagnosed with T2DM later on during the study. Based on the above results, the Applicant was asked to include a general warning in SmPC 4.4 for the potential risk of hypoglycaemia concerning the whole patient population (instead of the present warning which concerns the patient population with comorbid T2D). Applicant was also asked to amend the ADR table in the SmPC 4.8 with the finding on Hypoglycaemia AE in NT2DM subpopulation. Based on the Applicant's answer there is no clear additional risk for hypoglycaemia in NT2DM subjects thus no update of SmPC is needed. Additionally, the possible relation between hypoglycaemic events and age, diabetes duration, and HbA1c at baseline, has been explored, showing no clear differences. The effect of concomitant medication was also explored, and a considerably larger proportion of subjects in the semaglutide 2.4 mg group reporting level 2 or 3 hypoglycaemic episodes were treated with insulin and SU at baseline compared to those not reporting episodes (insulin: semaglutide vs placebo, 15.2% vs 8.5%; SU: semaglutide vs placebo, 42.4% vs 15.0%). Additionally, subjects who initiated insulin or SU during the study reported hypoglycaemic events more often. This increase in hypoglycaemia is known and already stated in the SmPC of semaglutide. The effect of the combination of semaglutide and DDP-4 in relation to hypoglycaemia has also been explored, which showed no clear effect on hypoglycaemia and no need to further update the SmPC. The relation between MASH status and hypoglycaemic events among T2D patients has also been also explored, with no relevant relation between MASH status and hypoglycaemia.

Diabetic retinopathy . Worsening of diabetic retinopathy is a known risk of semaglutide in patients with T2D, and patient with uncontrolled diabetic retinopathy or maculopathy were excluded from all trials. In study 4553, events of retinal disorders were reported at a comparable frequency on semaglutide vs placebo (3.1% vs 4.1%), most frequently concerning diabetic retinopathy, all nonproliferative, with no worsening of visual acuity, and age-related macular degeneration in one case on semaglutide, not likely related. Similar results were seen in supportive study 4296. These results are reassuring, though patients who may be at increased risk were excluded. The lack of data in MASHpatients with uncontrolled diabetic retinopathy is appropriately reported in section 4.4 of the SmPC.

Non-arteritic anterior ischaemic optic neuropathy (NAION). The PRAC has recently reviewed a signal suggesting a potential association between semaglutide and NAION, which it classified as a very rare adverse reaction. The SmPC has been updated accordingly. As for Eye disorders SOC, one Blurred vision AE and one Double vision AE were considered as related Eye disorder AEs in the semaglutide arm of ESSENCE study. Since blurred vision, visual impairment and decreased visual acuity might be the symptoms of NAION the applicant has clarified that subjects with T2D at baseline or who developed T2D during the trial were to undergo regular eye examinations according to the trial protocol. If a subject (with or without T2D) experienced visual impairment AEs during the trial, it was at the discretion of the investigator to follow up on the clinical sign or symptom as part of normal safety follow-up at each visit. As for eye examinations performed outside the clinical trial, the applicant would not have access to the findings, until it was reported as an adverse event. The Applicant also provided the numbers and frequencies of visual impairment-like AEs as requested. The intensity and time-course of these AEs were not indicative for NAION and their occurrence was balanced between the two treatment groups.

Immunogenicity . A low rate of ADAs without subjects with in vitro neutralising antibodies and no impact on safety was seen across studies in MASH subjects, in line with data on semaglutide for T2D and weight management.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

Ferritin. In trial 4553, the geometric mean values of ferritin decreased from baseline to week 72 for both semaglutide 2.4 mg and placebo, with ratio to baseline of 0,67 in the semaglutide group and 0,79 in the placebo group. As ferritin correlates to the iron-content of the body, the long-term risk of iron deficient anaemia cannot be excluded. Taking into account that semaglutide has gastrointestinal side effects which can cause malabsorption of nutrients including iron, the applicant was asked to show how haemoglobin varies depending on ferritin levels and give an explanation of the change of ferritin and if it can be related to hepatic functions. Haemoglobin levels seem to decrease until the 120 th  week, which might cause anaemia in the long term. The Applicant therefore was requested to provide graphical analyses of ferritin and haemoglobin over time. In addition, the Applicant was asked to formally assess the correlation between changes in ferritin and haemoglobin, including the possibility of a lag effect (i.e. that haemoglobin levels may follow ferritin changes with some delay). Based on these analyses, the Applicant was also asked to discuss the potential clinical implications for the risk of anaemia during long-term treatment. Ferritin levels remained in the normal ranges. Note that statistically significant conclusion cannot be made from the longer-term results due to the rather limited patient number beyond Week 120, and the decreasing ferritin levels still can be the sign of reduced inflammation and not the risk of slowly developing anaemia.

Changes in the mean Hb concentration is rather slight in both placebo and semaglutide groups, however a seemingly opposite trend can be observed in the semaglutide and in the placebo group from Week 72. This might suggest a delay in the decrease of the mean Hb concentration in the semaglutide group compared to the drop of the ferritin level which raises the possibility of the correlation between the ferritin and Hb decrease in the semaglutide group.

However, it was not considered as an important safety issue, so it is was not pursued further.

## Safety in special populations

MASH subgroups . In addition to safety data from pivotal study 4553 in 800 subjects with MASH F2 (n=174) and F3 (n=628) treated with semaglutide 2.4 mg, supportive data across stages of MASH come from study 4296 in 239 subjects with MASH F1 (n=68), F2 (n=50) and F3 (n=122) treated with semaglutide 0.1 mg, 0.2 mg or 0.4 mg, study 4492 in 47 subjects with MASH F4c treated semaglutide 2.4 mg, and study 4381 in 33 subjects with NAFLD with increased liver stiffness treated with semaglutide 0.4 mg OD. In general, a comparable safety profile was seen across subjects with MASH F1, F2, F3 and NAFLD with increased liver stiffness.

Specifically, though MASH compensated cirrhosis (fibrosis stage F4c) is not the intended target population for semaglutide 2.4 mg, patients with MASH and significant liver fibrosis may over time progress to compensated cirrhosis. While limited data is available (Study 4492) an no firm conclusions can be drawn, severe AEs were reported more frequently in MASH F4c patients (17%) compared to MASH F2 F3 patients (8.7%). However, the main events were related to GI AEs, which is a known risk, and no cluster of events was observed.

Baseline BMI . In study 4553, a total of 113, 115, 249 and 323 subjects with BMI &lt;27, 27-&lt;30, 30&lt;35, and &gt;=35 kg/m 2  were treated with semaglutide. AEs, SAEs and severe AEs were reported at comparable frequency across BMI ranges, but interruption or reduction of semaglutide dose was reported more frequently in subjects with BMI &lt; 27 kg/m 2 , in 21.2% vs 8.0%-13.7% and 21.2% vs 5.9%-12.2%. Most of the events triggering dose adjustments were related to GI AEs, which is expected given differences in exposure by BMI and the known safety profile of semaglutide. Differences in exposure due to low and high BMI are acknowledged and already reflected in section 5.2 of the SmPC in an adequate manner. Further, dose recommendations in section 4.2 of the SmPC already mention the need of dose escalation, including the likelihood of significant gastrointestinal symptoms and the possibility of delaying the dose escalation or lowering the dose according to these symptoms, which is considered sufficient to guide a personalised approach for each patient.

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

In study 4553, a comparable safety profile was seen when analysed separately in subjects with or without T2D, or across race. When analysed across sex, AEs were reported at comparable frequency by female and male subjects, and most events are considered unlikely related to study drug treatment. No additional warnings by sex are considered necessary.

The thyroid function is commonly affected in metabolic syndrome, and hepatic hypothyroidism is known as an important underlying pathophysiology in NASH/MASH. TSH level wasn't tested neither in the screening period nor during visits, however, in total 115 subjects (14,4%) had hypothyroidism at baseline and further 44 subjects had other thyroid-related illnesses. 120 (15%) subjects were already on levothyroxine medication. The Applicant was therefore asked to provide a subgroup analysis of safety parameters (overall safety, AEs by SOC and PTs, SAEs, AESIs) comparing subjects with hypothytroidism to non-hypothyroid subjects. According to the Applicant's response, slight but clear differences can be seen for hypothyroid patients compared to the non-hypothyroid ones. However, these differences are not consistent over the whole AE spectrum, and they do not seem to be clinically meaningful.

Age . In study 4553, a substantial proportion of subjects randomised to semaglutide 2.4 mg were aged &gt;= 65 years (26.3%). AEs were reported at similar frequency across age-groups &lt;45, 45-64, and &gt;=65 years of age, in ~85% of subjects, leading to dose reduction in a higher proportion of patients &gt;=65 years (13.8% vs ~8.5%) and discontinuation of semaglutide in 3.3% vs 0.8% at age &lt;45 years. A higher proportion of subjects &gt;= 65 years of age on semaglutide reported SAEs, in 14.8% at age &gt;=65 years vs 10.8% &lt; 45 years, and severe AEs (11.9% vs 5.0%). Decreased appetite was reported more frequently in subjects &gt;= 65 years of age as compared with younger age groups, and as compared with placebo, which may result in (unwanted) weight loss and loss of muscle mass. At higher age, patients may benefit less from long-term beneficial effects with regard to progression of MASH due to a more limited life expectancy and may be more at risk due to unwanted adverse effects like loss of weight or muscle mass. However, this is part of the mechanism of action of semaglutide and well-known in clinical practice. Updates in the SmPC were not considered necessary. In the current application, data from the pivotal study 4553 on treatment for 72 weeks are provided. Final results of study 4553 will be submitted after completion of the planned 240 weeks of treatment, providing relevant additional safety data, including data on several safety risks that are expected to persist (like hypoglycaemia) or increase (like cholelithiasis and acute pancreatitis) during long-term treatment.
